\chapter{Stochastic Quantization and Singular SPDE}
\label{ch:constructive-stochastic-quantization-singular-spde}

\ConventionsEuclidean

Stochastic quantization constructs Euclidean QFT data as the invariant law of
a Markov process on fields.  The auxiliary time of the process is not
Lorentzian time and is not an extra physical dimension.  Its purpose is to
replace a formal functional integral by a dynamical construction whose
stationary distribution can sometimes be proved to exist.

In singular dimensions the stochastic equation itself must be renormalized.
The counterterms are local coordinates in the regulated stochastic drift and
must be fixed before the cutoff is removed.  The resulting invariant measure
still has to be checked against the OS hypotheses before it becomes a
Lorentzian QFT by reconstruction.

\section{Finite-Dimensional Langevin Identity}

\paragraph{Finite-dimensional invariant density.}
Let \(S:\R^n\to\R\) be smooth and confining enough that
\[
  Z=\int_{\R^n}\ee^{-S(x)}\,\dd x<\infty .
\]
The overdamped Langevin equation
\begin{equation}
  \dd X_t=-\nabla S(X_t)\,\dd t+\sqrt2\,\dd B_t
  \label{eq:finite-dimensional-langevin}
\end{equation}
has generator
\[
  Lf=-\nabla S\cdot\nabla f+\Delta f .
\]
For smooth compactly supported \(f,g\),
\begin{equation}
  \int (Lf)g\,\ee^{-S}\dd x
  =
  -\int \nabla f\cdot\nabla g\,\ee^{-S}\dd x .
  \label{eq:finite-dimensional-dirichlet-form}
\end{equation}
Therefore \(L\) is symmetric in \(L^2(\ee^{-S}\dd x)\), and the probability
measure \(Z^{-1}\ee^{-S}\dd x\) is invariant.
The calculation is ordinary finite-dimensional integration by parts:
\[
  \int (-\partial_iS)(\partial_i f)g\,\ee^{-S}\dd x
  =
  \int \partial_i(\ee^{-S})(\partial_i f)g\,\dd x .
\]
Adding the Laplacian contribution and integrating by parts once more,
\[
  \int
  \bigl[\partial_i(\ee^{-S})(\partial_i f)g
        +(\partial_i^2 f)g\ee^{-S}\bigr]\dd x
  =
  -\int (\partial_i f)(\partial_i g)\ee^{-S}\dd x .
\]
Summing over \(i\) gives \eqref{eq:finite-dimensional-dirichlet-form}.  With
\(g=1\), the right-hand side vanishes, so the adjoint generator annihilates
\(\ee^{-S}\dd x\).  This is the infinitesimal invariant-density identity;
actual stationarity of the stochastic process is the semigroup statement in
Lemma~\ref{lem:finite-cutoff-langevin-reversibility}.

\begin{lemma}[Finite-cutoff reversibility from the gradient Langevin datum]
\label{lem:finite-cutoff-langevin-reversibility}
Let
\[
  \dd\pi(x)=Z^{-1}\ee^{-S(x)}\dd x .
\]
Assume that the symmetric operator
\[
  L_0 f=-\nabla S\cdot\nabla f+\Delta f,\qquad
  f\in C_c^\infty(\R^n),
\]
is essentially self-adjoint as an operator in \(L^2(\pi)\), and that its
self-adjoint closure \(\overline L\) generates a conservative Markov
semigroup
\[
  P_t=\ee^{t\overline L},\qquad t\ge0,
\]
on \(L^2(\pi)\).  Then \(P_t\) is reversible with respect to \(\pi\):
\begin{equation}
  \int (P_t f)g\,\dd\pi
  =
  \int f(P_t g)\,\dd\pi ,
  \qquad f,g\in L^2(\pi),
  \label{eq:finite-cutoff-langevin-reversibility}
\end{equation}
and \(\pi\) is invariant:
\begin{equation}
  \int P_t f\,\dd\pi=\int f\,\dd\pi ,
  \qquad f\in L^2(\pi)\cap L^1(\pi).
  \label{eq:finite-cutoff-langevin-semigroup-invariance}
\end{equation}
For \(f,g\in C_c^\infty(\R^n)\), the Dirichlet form of the closure is the
closure of
\[
  \mathcal E(f,g)
  =
  \int \nabla f\cdot\nabla g\,\dd\pi .
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Equation~\eqref{eq:finite-dimensional-dirichlet-form} says precisely that
\[
  \langle L_0 f,g\rangle_{L^2(\pi)}
  =
  -\mathcal E(f,g)
  =
  \langle f,L_0 g\rangle_{L^2(\pi)}
\]
on the core \(C_c^\infty(\R^n)\).  Hence the self-adjoint closure
\(\overline L\) is nonpositive.  By the spectral theorem,
\(\ee^{t\overline L}\) is a self-adjoint contraction on \(L^2(\pi)\), which
gives \eqref{eq:finite-cutoff-langevin-reversibility}.  Conservativeness is
the statement \(P_t1=1\).  Taking \(g=1\) in
\eqref{eq:finite-cutoff-langevin-reversibility} gives
\[
  \int P_t f\,\dd\pi
  =
  \langle P_t f,1\rangle
  =
  \langle f,P_t1\rangle
  =
  \int f\,\dd\pi ,
\]
first for bounded \(L^2\) functions and then for
\(L^1(\pi)\cap L^2(\pi)\) by truncation.  The Dirichlet form statement is the
definition of the closed quadratic form associated with the nonpositive
self-adjoint operator \(\overline L\), restricted to the displayed core
where the integration-by-parts calculation identifies the form explicitly.
\end{proof}

The lemma records the finite-regulator theorem actually used below.  The
integration-by-parts identity proves the symmetric Dirichlet form on a core;
the passage to a stationary Markov process also requires the finite-dimensional
diffusion to be conservative and the generator closure to produce the
semigroup.  These hypotheses are elementary for the smooth compact cutoff
systems used as finite regulators, but they are logically distinct from the
singular infinite-dimensional limit.

\section{Regularized Field Equation}

Let \(M\) be a compact Euclidean spatial manifold, let \(C_\Lambda\) be a
smooth ultraviolet cutoff covariance on \(M\), and let \(\xi_\Lambda\) be
white noise in stochastic time with spatial covariance chosen so that the
linear equation has invariant Gaussian law \(\mu_{C_\Lambda}\).  A
regularized scalar \(\Phi^4\) Langevin equation has the form
\begin{equation}
  \partial_\tau\phi_\Lambda
  =
  -C_\Lambda^{-1}\phi_\Lambda
  -\lambda_\Lambda\phi_\Lambda^3
  +c_\Lambda\phi_\Lambda
  +\xi_\Lambda .
  \label{eq:regularized-spde-gradient-flow}
\end{equation}
The drift is the negative \(L^2\)-gradient of the regulated action
\begin{equation}
  S_\Lambda(\phi)
  =
  \frac12(\phi,C_\Lambda^{-1}\phi)
  +
  \int_M
  \left[
    \frac{\lambda_\Lambda}{4}\phi^4
    -\frac{c_\Lambda}{2}\phi^2
  \right]\dd^dx .
  \label{eq:regularized-spde-action}
\end{equation}
At finite \(\Lambda\), the argument of the previous section applies on the
finite-dimensional cutoff field space.  Thus
\[
  Z_\Lambda^{-1}\ee^{-S_\Lambda(\phi)}D\phi
\]
is invariant.  The constructive question is whether the laws of
\(\phi_\Lambda\), with tuned local coordinates, converge as
\(\Lambda\to\infty\).

\section{Renormalized Products}

For the Gaussian free field in spatial dimension \(d\geq2\), \(\phi(x)\) is
a distribution.  Products in the drift are therefore defined through
renormalized local functionals.  If
\(\phi_\epsilon=\rho_\epsilon\ast\phi\) and
\[
  C_\epsilon=\mathbb E[\phi_\epsilon(x)^2],
\]
then
\begin{equation}
  :\!\phi_\epsilon^3\!:
  =
  \phi_\epsilon^3-3C_\epsilon\phi_\epsilon .
  \label{eq:spde-cubic-wick-product}
\end{equation}
The coefficient \(3\) is not conventional: it counts the possible
contractions of two of the three fields.  The same convention gives
\[
  :\!\phi_\epsilon^4\!:
  =
  \phi_\epsilon^4-6C_\epsilon\phi_\epsilon^2+3C_\epsilon^2 .
\]

\section{Stationary Stochastic Convolution and Wick Powers}

The first analytic object in the Da Prato--Debussche construction is the
stationary solution of the linear stochastic heat equation.  We record the
Fourier construction because it fixes the normalization of the noise and
proves, in a Sobolev form, the existence of the Wick powers used later.

Let \(\mathbb T^2=\R^2/(2\pi\mathbb Z)^2\), and let
\(\{e_k\}_{k\in\mathbb Z^2}\) be the orthonormal Fourier basis of
\(L^2(\mathbb T^2)\), normalized so that
\(-\Delta e_k=|k|^2e_k\).  The spacetime white noise in the stochastic
quantization equation is normalized by
\[
  \mathbb E[\xi(\tau,x)\xi(\tau',y)]
  =
  2\,\delta(\tau-\tau')\delta(x-y).
\]
Equivalently, in Fourier coordinates,
\begin{equation}
  \dd X_k(\tau)
  =
  -(|k|^2+m^2)X_k(\tau)\dd\tau+\sqrt2\,\dd B_k(\tau),
  \qquad X_{-k}=\overline{X_k},
  \label{eq:spde-fourier-ou-mode}
\end{equation}
where the complex Brownian motions are paired so that the reconstructed
field is real.

\begin{lemma}[Stationary covariance of the linear SPDE]
\label{lem:spde-stationary-ou-covariance}
The stationary solution of \eqref{eq:spde-fourier-ou-mode} satisfies
\begin{equation}
  \mathbb E[X_k\overline{X_\ell}]
  =
  \frac{\delta_{k\ell}}{|k|^2+m^2}.
  \label{eq:spde-stationary-fourier-covariance}
\end{equation}
Consequently \(X(\tau,\cdot)\) has the massive Gaussian free-field law with
covariance \((-\Delta+m^2)^{-1}\), and
\begin{equation}
  \mathbb E\|X(\tau)\|_{H^{-s}(\mathbb T^2)}^2
  =
  \sum_{k\in\mathbb Z^2}
  \frac{(1+|k|^2)^{-s}}{|k|^2+m^2}
  <\infty
  \qquad(s>0).
  \label{eq:spde-ou-negative-sobolev-bound}
\end{equation}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
For the real one-dimensional Ornstein--Uhlenbeck equation
\(\dd Z_t=-aZ_t\dd t+\sqrt2\,\dd B_t\), \(a>0\), the stationary solution is
obtained by solving from time \(-\infty\):
\[
  Z_t=\sqrt2\int_{-\infty}^{t}e^{-a(t-r)}\,\dd B_r,
\]
where the integral is well-defined in \(L^2(\Omega)\) because
\(\int_0^\infty e^{-2au}\dd u<\infty\).  Ito isometry gives
\[
  \mathbb E|Z_t|^2
  =
  2\int_0^\infty e^{-2au}\,\dd u
  =
  a^{-1}.
\]
For the complex Fourier mode \(X_k\) one applies the same calculation to the
paired real and imaginary coordinates, with the covariance convention in
\eqref{eq:spde-fourier-ou-mode} chosen so that
\(\mathbb E[X_k\overline{X_\ell}]=0\) for \(k\ne\ell\) and
\(\mathbb E|X_k|^2=(|k|^2+m^2)^{-1}\).  Taking
\(a=|k|^2+m^2\) gives \eqref{eq:spde-stationary-fourier-covariance}.

For a finite Fourier cutoff \(N\), Parseval gives
\[
  \mathbb E\|P_NX(\tau)\|_{H^{-s}}^2
  =
  \sum_{|k|\le N}
  \frac{(1+|k|^2)^{-s}}{|k|^2+m^2}.
\]
The summands are nonnegative, so monotone convergence passes to the infinite
sum.  In two dimensions the large-\(|k|\) summand is comparable to
\(|k|^{-2-2s}\), and the lattice count
\(\#\{k\in\mathbb Z^2: R\le |k|<R+1\}=O(R)\) reduces convergence to
\(\sum_R R^{-1-2s}<\infty\), namely \(s>0\).  Thus the stationary field is an
\(H^{-s}\)-valued Gaussian random distribution for every \(s>0\), with
covariance \((-\Delta+m^2)^{-1}\).
\end{proof}

Let \(P_N\) be the Fourier projection to \(|k|\leq N\), set \(X_N=P_NX\),
and write
\[
  C_N(x-y)=\mathbb E[X_N(x)X_N(y)].
\]
The Wick powers are
\[
  :\!X_N^2\!:=X_N^2-C_N(0),\qquad
  :\!X_N^3\!:=X_N^3-3C_N(0)X_N .
\]

\begin{lemma}[Two-dimensional massive-convolution bound]
\label{lem:spde-massive-convolution-bound}
Let
\[
  g(k)=\frac{1}{1+|k|^2},\qquad
  L(k)=1+\log(2+|k|),
  \qquad k\in\mathbb Z^2 .
\]
For every integer \(n\geq1\) there is \(C_n<\infty\) such that the
\(n\)-fold lattice convolution satisfies
\begin{equation}
  g^{*n}(q)
  \leq
  C_n\,\frac{L(q)^{n-1}}{1+|q|^2},
  \qquad q\in\mathbb Z^2 .
  \label{eq:spde-lattice-convolution-bound}
\end{equation}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
The case \(n=1\) is the definition.  The induction step is the elementary
estimate
\begin{equation}
  \sum_{p\in\mathbb Z^2}
  \frac{L(p)^a}{(1+|p|^2)(1+|q-p|^2)}
  \leq
  C_a\,\frac{L(q)^{a+1}}{1+|q|^2},
  \qquad a\geq0 .
  \label{eq:spde-convolution-induction-estimate}
\end{equation}
For \(|q|\leq4\) the left side is finite and the estimate is absorbed into
the constant.  Assume \(|q|>4\).  Split the sum into three regions:
\[
  A=\{|p|\leq |q|/2\},\qquad
  B=\{|q-p|\leq |q|/2\},\qquad
  C=(A\cup B)^c .
\]
On \(A\), \(1+|q-p|^2\geq c(1+|q|^2)\), and
\[
  \sum_{|p|\leq |q|/2}\frac{L(p)^a}{1+|p|^2}
  \leq
  C_a L(q)^{a+1},
\]
because the two-dimensional shell of radius \(r\) contributes
\(\lesssim r^{-1}(1+\log r)^a\).  This proves the desired bound on \(A\).
The region \(B\) is identical after replacing \(r=q-p\): then
\(|p|\geq |q|/2\), \(L(p)\leq C L(q)\), and the remaining
\(\sum_{|r|\leq |q|/2}(1+|r|^2)^{-1}\) contributes one additional
logarithm.
On \(C\), both \(|p|\) and \(|q-p|\) are \(\geq |q|/2\).  Decompose \(C\)
into dyadic annuli \(|p|\sim 2^j|q|\) and
\(|q-p|\sim 2^k|q|\).  The number of lattice points in a two-dimensional
dyadic annulus of radius \(R\) is \(O(R^2)\), while the summand is bounded
by \(C L(p)^a/(|p|^2|q-p|^2)\).  Summing first over the smaller of the two
annuli and then over the larger gives
\[
  \sum_{p\in C}
  \frac{L(p)^a}{(1+|p|^2)(1+|q-p|^2)}
  \leq
  C_a\frac{L(q)^a}{1+|q|^2}.
\]
Combining the three regions proves
\eqref{eq:spde-convolution-induction-estimate}.  Applying it with
\(a=n-1\) to \(g*g^{*n}\) proves
\eqref{eq:spde-lattice-convolution-bound} by induction.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Smeared Wick powers of the two-dimensional stochastic convolution]
\label{prop:spde-two-dimensional-wick-powers}
For every \(n\geq1\) and every \(f\in C^\infty(\mathbb T^2)\), the random
variables
\[
  :\!X_N^n\!:(f)=\int_{\mathbb T^2}f(x):\!X_N(x)^n\!:\,\dd^2x
\]
converge in \(L^2(\Omega)\) as \(N\to\infty\).  Moreover, for every
\(s>0\), the fields \(:\!X_N^n\!:\) converge in
\(L^2(\Omega;H^{-s}(\mathbb T^2))\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
The Wick product covariance is the finite-dimensional Gaussian identity
\begin{equation}
  \mathbb E\!\left[
    :\!X_N(x)^n\!:\,:\!X_M(y)^n\!:
  \right]
  =
  n!\,C_{N,M}(x-y)^n,
  \qquad
  C_{N,M}(x-y)=\mathbb E[X_N(x)X_M(y)] .
  \label{eq:spde-wick-power-covariance}
\end{equation}
This follows from the generating function
\(\exp(tX_N-\frac12t^2C_N(0))\) and differentiating \(n\) times in each
variable.  Hence
\[
  \mathbb E\left[
    :\!X_N^n\!:(f)\,:\!X_M^n\!:(f)
  \right]
  =
  n!\int_{\mathbb T^2\times\mathbb T^2}
  f(x)f(y)\,C_{N,M}(x-y)^n\,\dd^2x\,\dd^2y .
\]
The massive two-dimensional covariance has the local form
\[
  C(x)=-\frac{1}{2\pi}\log |x|+O(1)
  \qquad (x\to0),
\]
and powers of \(\log |x|\) are locally integrable in two dimensions:
\[
  \int_0^r \rho\,|\log \rho|^n\,\dd\rho<\infty .
\]
The kernels \(C_{N,M}\) converge to \(C\) away from the diagonal, and the
standard Fourier cutoff bounds are dominated by an integrable logarithmic
majorant.  Dominated convergence therefore proves the \(L^2\)-Cauchy
property for every smeared Wick power.

For the Sobolev statement, use the dual norm
\[
  \|u\|_{H^{-s}}^2
  =
  \sum_{q\in\mathbb Z^2}
  (1+|q|^2)^{-s}|\widehat u(q)|^2 .
\]
Applying \eqref{eq:spde-wick-power-covariance} to Fourier test functions
shows that
\begin{equation}
  \mathbb E|\widehat{:\!X_N^n\!:}(q)|^2
  =
  n!\!
  \sum_{\substack{k_1+\cdots+k_n=q\\ |k_i|\leq N}}
  \prod_{i=1}^n\frac{1}{|k_i|^2+m^2}.
  \label{eq:spde-wick-fourier-variance-convolution}
\end{equation}
The right side is bounded by
\(C_{n,m}L(q)^{n-1}(1+|q|^2)^{-1}\) by
Lemma~\ref{lem:spde-massive-convolution-bound}.  Therefore
\[
  \sum_{q\in\mathbb Z^2}
  (1+|q|^2)^{-s}
  \mathbb E|\widehat{:\!X_N^n\!:}(q)|^2
  \leq
  C_{n,m}
  \sum_{q\in\mathbb Z^2}
  \frac{L(q)^{n-1}}{(1+|q|^2)^{1+s}}<\infty
\]
for every \(s>0\).  For fixed \(q\), the cutoff variances and mixed
covariances converge by dominated convergence of the convolution sums.
The same convolution bound dominates
\(\mathbb E|\widehat{:\!X_N^n\!:}(q)
          -\widehat{:\!X_M^n\!:}(q)|^2\), up to a harmless constant, so
dominated convergence over \(q\) proves convergence in
\(L^2(\Omega;H^{-s})\).  In particular the stochastic input needed by the
Sobolev DPD fixed point below is available for any prescribed
\(0<\kappa<1/4\) after taking \(s=\kappa\).
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Smooth Fourier multiplier covariance convergence]
\label{prop:spde-smooth-fourier-multiplier-covariance-convergence}
Let \(m^2>0\), and let
\[
  C(z)=\sum_{k\in\mathbb Z^2}\frac{e^{ik\cdot z}}{|k|^2+m^2}
\]
be the massive covariance on \(\mathbb T^2\), understood through the
dyadic Fourier decomposition used below.  For
\(\sigma\in\{A,B\}\), let \(\mu^\sigma\in C_c^\infty(\mathbb R^2)\) be
real, even, and satisfy \(\mu^\sigma(0)=1\).  Define the cutoff Gaussian
field on the same Gaussian probability space by
\[
  X_N^\sigma=\mu^\sigma(D/N)X,
\]
so that the mixed and cutoff covariances are translation invariant kernels
\[
  C_N^{\sigma\infty}(z)
  =
  \sum_{k\in\mathbb Z^2}
  \frac{\mu^\sigma(k/N)}{|k|^2+m^2}e^{ik\cdot z},
  \qquad
  C_N^{\sigma\sigma}(z)
  =
  \sum_{k\in\mathbb Z^2}
  \frac{\mu^\sigma(k/N)^2}{|k|^2+m^2}e^{ik\cdot z}.
\]
Then, for every \(1\leq p<\infty\),
\begin{equation}
  C_N^{\sigma\infty}\longrightarrow C,
  \qquad
  C_N^{\sigma\sigma}\longrightarrow C
  \quad\hbox{in }L^p(\mathbb T^2).
  \label{eq:spde-smooth-fourier-multiplier-covariance-lp}
\end{equation}
Consequently the hypotheses of
Lemma~\ref{lem:spde-mixed-covariance-common-wick-limits} hold for
every finite Wick order \(n\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Choose a smooth radial dyadic partition of unity on frequency space,
\[
  \chi_{-1}(\xi)+\sum_{j\geq0}\chi_j(\xi)=1,
  \qquad
  \chi_j(\xi)=\chi(2^{-j}\xi),
\]
where \(\chi_{-1}\) is supported near the origin and \(\chi\) is supported
in an annulus.  Let
\[
  a(k)=|k|^2+m^2,
\]
and let \(\nu_N(k)\) denote any one of the three multipliers
\[
  1,\qquad \mu^\sigma(k/N),\qquad \mu^\sigma(k/N)^2 .
\]
For \(j\geq0\), set
\[
  K_{j,N}^{\nu}(z)
  =
  \sum_{k\in\mathbb Z^2}
  \chi_j(k)\frac{\nu_N(k)}{a(k)}e^{ik\cdot z}.
\]
We first prove the uniform dyadic kernel bound
\begin{equation}
  |K_{j,N}^{\nu}(z)|
  \leq
  C_M\bigl(1+2^j d_{\mathbb T^2}(z,0)\bigr)^{-M},
  \qquad M\geq0,
  \label{eq:spde-smooth-multiplier-dyadic-kernel-bound}
\end{equation}
with a constant independent of \(j,N,\nu\).  Indeed, put
\[
  q_{j,N}^{\nu}(\xi)
  =
  \chi_j(\xi)\frac{\nu_N(\xi)}{|\xi|^2+m^2}.
\]
On the support of \(\chi_j\), \(|\xi|\simeq2^j\).  If
\(\nu_N(\xi)=\mu^\sigma(\xi/N)\) or \(\mu^\sigma(\xi/N)^2\), the support
of \(q_{j,N}^{\nu}\) is empty unless \(2^j\leq C N\); on the nonempty
support, derivatives of \(\nu_N\) satisfy
\[
  |\partial^\alpha\nu_N(\xi)|\leq C_\alpha N^{-|\alpha|}
  \leq C_\alpha 2^{-j|\alpha|}.
\]
Together with the corresponding derivative bounds for
\(\chi_j\) and \((|\xi|^2+m^2)^{-1}\), this gives
\begin{equation}
  |\partial^\alpha q_{j,N}^{\nu}(\xi)|
  \leq
  C_\alpha\,2^{-2j}2^{-j|\alpha|}.
  \label{eq:spde-smooth-multiplier-symbol-derivatives}
\end{equation}
Let \(\check q_{j,N}^{\nu}\) be the inverse Fourier transform on
\(\mathbb R^2\).  Integrating by parts in the oscillatory integral and
using \eqref{eq:spde-smooth-multiplier-symbol-derivatives} gives
\[
  |\check q_{j,N}^{\nu}(x)|
  \leq
  C_M(1+2^j|x|)^{-M}.
\]
Poisson summation identifies \(K_{j,N}^{\nu}\) with the periodization of
\(\check q_{j,N}^{\nu}\).  Summing over lattice translates gives
\eqref{eq:spde-smooth-multiplier-dyadic-kernel-bound} when \(M>2\);
decreasing \(M\) if necessary gives the stated form for all \(M\geq0\).
The low-frequency block \(j=-1\) is bounded uniformly because it contains
only finitely many lattice modes.

Summing \eqref{eq:spde-smooth-multiplier-dyadic-kernel-bound} over \(j\)
with \(M>2\) gives the logarithmic majorant
\begin{equation}
  \left|
    \sum_{j\geq-1}K_{j,N}^{\nu}(z)
  \right|
  \leq
  C\bigl(1+|\log d_{\mathbb T^2}(z,0)|\bigr),
  \label{eq:spde-smooth-multiplier-log-majorant}
\end{equation}
where the right side is interpreted as a bounded function away from
\(z=0\).  The logarithmic singularity belongs to
\(L^p(\mathbb T^2)\) for every finite \(p\), because in polar coordinates
\[
  \int_0^{1/2} r\,|\log r|^p\,\dd r<\infty .
\]

It remains to prove pointwise convergence away from the diagonal.  Fix
\(z\neq0\).  For any fixed dyadic level \(j\), the block
\(K_{j,N}^{\nu}\) is a finite sum, and the multiplier
\(\nu_N(k)\) tends to \(1\) for every fixed \(k\).  Hence
\[
  K_{j,N}^{\nu}(z)\longrightarrow K_{j,\infty}(z)
\]
for each fixed \(j\).  The tail is uniform in \(N\):
\[
  \sum_{j>J}|K_{j,N}^{\nu}(z)|
  \leq
  C_M\sum_{j>J}(1+2^jd_{\mathbb T^2}(z,0))^{-M}
  \leq
  C_{M,z}\,2^{-JM}.
\]
Therefore the full dyadic sums converge pointwise for every \(z\neq0\).
Together with the logarithmic majorant
\eqref{eq:spde-smooth-multiplier-log-majorant}, dominated convergence gives
\eqref{eq:spde-smooth-fourier-multiplier-covariance-lp}.  Taking
\(\nu_N=\mu^\sigma(k/N)\) gives the mixed covariance, while taking
\(\nu_N=\mu^\sigma(k/N)^2\) gives the cutoff covariance.  Since this holds
for \(p=n\), and since a translation-invariant kernel \(K(x-y)\) has
\(L^n(\mathbb T^2\times\mathbb T^2)\)-norm equal to the volume factor times
its \(L^n(\mathbb T^2)\)-norm, the hypotheses of
Lemma~\ref{lem:spde-mixed-covariance-common-wick-limits} hold for
every finite Wick order \(n\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Mixed-covariance criterion for common Wick limits]
\label{lem:spde-mixed-covariance-common-wick-limits}
Let \(X\) be a centered Gaussian generalized field on \(\mathbb T^2\), and
let \(X_N^\sigma\), \(\sigma\in\{A,B\}\), be centered Gaussian cutoff fields
on the same probability space.  Write
\[
  C(x,y)=\mathbb E[X(x)X(y)],
  \qquad
  C_N^{\sigma\sigma}(x,y)
  =
  \mathbb E[X_N^\sigma(x)X_N^\sigma(y)],
\]
and
\[
  C_N^{\sigma\infty}(x,y)
  =
  \mathbb E[X_N^\sigma(x)X(y)] .
\]
Fix \(n\geq1\), and assume that these covariance kernels are represented by
functions in \(L^n(\mathbb T^2\times\mathbb T^2)\) and that, for
\(\sigma=A,B\),
\begin{equation}
  \|C_N^{\sigma\sigma}-C\|_{L^n(\mathbb T^2\times\mathbb T^2)}
  +
  \|C_N^{\sigma\infty}-C\|_{L^n(\mathbb T^2\times\mathbb T^2)}
  \longrightarrow0 .
  \label{eq:spde-mixed-covariance-common-wick-limits}
\end{equation}
Then, for every real \(f\in C^\infty(\mathbb T^2)\),
\begin{equation}
  :\!(X_N^\sigma)^n\!:(f)
  \longrightarrow
  :\!X^n\!:(f)
  \quad\hbox{in }L^2(\Omega),
  \qquad \sigma=A,B,
  \label{eq:spde-mixed-covariance-wick-coordinate-convergence}
\end{equation}
where each Wick product is normal ordered with respect to the covariance of
the Gaussian field appearing in it.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Let
\[
  Y_N^\sigma=:\!(X_N^\sigma)^n\!:(f),
  \qquad
  Y=:\!X^n\!:(f).
\]
For two jointly Gaussian centered random variables \(U,V\), Wick ordering
with respect to the individual variances gives
\[
  \mathbb E\bigl[:\!U^n\!:\,:\!V^n\!:\bigr]
  =
  n!\,\mathbb E[UV]^n .
\]
This follows by differentiating the identity
\[
  \mathbb E\!\left[
    e^{tU-\frac12t^2\mathbb E[U^2]}
    e^{sV-\frac12s^2\mathbb E[V^2]}
  \right]
  =
  e^{ts\mathbb E[UV]}
\]
\(n\) times in \(t\) and \(n\) times in \(s\) at the origin.  Therefore
\[
\begin{aligned}
  \mathbb E|Y_N^\sigma-Y|^2
  =
  n!\int_{\mathbb T^2\times\mathbb T^2}
  f(x)f(y)
  \Big[
    \big(C_N^{\sigma\sigma}(x,y)\big)^n
    -2\big(C_N^{\sigma\infty}(x,y)\big)^n
    +C(x,y)^n
  \Big]\,\dd^2x\,\dd^2y .
\end{aligned}
\]
It remains to show that the two \(n\)-th powers converge in \(L^1\) to
\(C^n\).  If \(a,b\in L^n(\mathbb T^2\times\mathbb T^2)\), then
\[
  |a^n-b^n|
  \leq
  n|a-b|\bigl(|a|^{n-1}+|b|^{n-1}\bigr).
\]
Holder's inequality gives
\[
  \|a^n-b^n\|_{L^1}
  \leq
  n\|a-b\|_{L^n}
  \left(
    \|a\|_{L^n}^{n-1}+\|b\|_{L^n}^{n-1}
  \right).
\]
The convergence assumption
\eqref{eq:spde-mixed-covariance-common-wick-limits} implies that the
\(L^n\)-norms of \(C_N^{\sigma\sigma}\) and
\(C_N^{\sigma\infty}\) are bounded and that both kernels converge to \(C\)
in \(L^n\).  Hence
\[
  \big(C_N^{\sigma\sigma}\big)^n\to C^n,
  \qquad
  \big(C_N^{\sigma\infty}\big)^n\to C^n
  \quad\hbox{in }L^1.
\]
Since \(f\) is bounded, the displayed formula for
\(\mathbb E|Y_N^\sigma-Y|^2\) tends to zero.  This proves
\eqref{eq:spde-mixed-covariance-wick-coordinate-convergence}.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Common limits of finite Wick-coordinate vectors]
\label{rem:spde-common-wick-coordinate-limits}
Fix finitely many pairs
\[
  (n_j,f_j),\qquad 1\leq j\leq r,
\]
where \(n_j\geq1\) and \(f_j\in C^\infty(\mathbb T^2)\).  For one cutoff
sequence \(X_N\), set
\[
  \mathcal X_N
  =
  \bigl(:\!X_N^{n_1}\!:(f_1),\ldots,
        :\!X_N^{n_r}\!:(f_r)\bigr)\in\R^r .
\]
Then \(\mathcal X_N\) converges in \(L^2(\Omega;\R^r)\), hence in
probability, to
\[
  \mathcal X
  =
  \bigl(:\!X^{n_1}\!:(f_1),\ldots,
        :\!X^{n_r}\!:(f_r)\bigr).
\]

More generally, suppose \(X_N^A\) and \(X_N^B\) are two cutoff
approximations of the same massive Gaussian field on a common probability
space, and suppose that, for every \(j\) and
\(\sigma\in\{A,B\}\),
\begin{equation}
  :\!(X_N^\sigma)^{n_j}\!:(f_j)
  \longrightarrow
  :\!X^{n_j}\!:(f_j)
  \quad\hbox{in }L^2(\Omega).
  \label{eq:spde-common-wick-coordinate-l2-hypothesis}
\end{equation}
Then the coordinate vectors
\[
  \mathcal X_N^\sigma
  =
  \bigl(:\!(X_N^\sigma)^{n_1}\!:(f_1),\ldots,
        :\!(X_N^\sigma)^{n_r}\!:(f_r)\bigr)
\]
satisfy
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal X_N^\sigma\longrightarrow\mathcal X
  \quad\hbox{in }L^2(\Omega;\R^r)
  \quad\hbox{and in probability},
  \qquad \sigma=A,B .
  \label{eq:spde-common-wick-coordinate-vector-convergence}
\end{equation}
For every bounded continuous \(F:\R^r\to\C\),
\begin{equation}
  F(\mathcal X_N^\sigma)\longrightarrow F(\mathcal X)
  \quad\hbox{in probability}.
  \label{eq:spde-common-wick-coordinate-observable-convergence}
\end{equation}
For the single Fourier cutoff sequence, each coordinate converges in
\(L^2(\Omega)\) by
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-two-dimensional-wick-powers}.  Therefore
\[
  \mathbb E|\mathcal X_N-\mathcal X|_{\R^r}^2
  =
  \sum_{j=1}^r
  \mathbb E
  \left|
    :\!X_N^{n_j}\!:(f_j)-:\!X^{n_j}\!:(f_j)
  \right|^2
  \longrightarrow0 .
\]
The two-regulator statement is identical after replacing the preceding
coordinate convergence by
\eqref{eq:spde-common-wick-coordinate-l2-hypothesis}; a concrete covariance
criterion for this hypothesis is
Lemma~\ref{lem:spde-mixed-covariance-common-wick-limits}.
\(L^2\)-convergence
implies convergence in probability by Chebyshev's inequality.  Finally,
if \(\mathcal X_N^\sigma\to\mathcal X\) in probability and \(F\) is
continuous, then \(F(\mathcal X_N^\sigma)\to F(\mathcal X)\) in probability
because the inverse image of the complement of any small neighborhood of
\(F(\mathcal X)\) is controlled by continuity of \(F\) at the realized value
of \(\mathcal X\); equivalently, every almost surely convergent subsequence
has a further subsequence on which the conclusion follows pointwise.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Convergence of the Wick quartic potential]
\label{rem:spde-wick-quartic-potential-convergence}
Let \(\lambda\in\R\), and define the cutoff Wick quartic functional on the
Gaussian probability space of
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-two-dimensional-wick-powers} by
\[
  V_N=\frac{\lambda}{4}\int_{\mathbb T^2}:\!X_N(x)^4\!:\,\dd^2x
  =
  \frac{\lambda}{4}\,:\!X_N^4\!:(1).
\]
Then there is an \(L^2(\Omega)\) random variable
\[
  V=\frac{\lambda}{4}\,:\!X^4\!:(1)
\]
such that \(V_N\to V\) in \(L^2(\Omega)\), and hence in probability.

More generally, suppose \(X_N^A\) and \(X_N^B\) are two cutoff
approximations of the same massive Gaussian field for which the conclusion
of Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-two-dimensional-wick-powers} holds for
\(:\!(X_N^\sigma)^4\!:(1)\), \(\sigma=A,B\), with the same limiting Wick
functional \(:\!X^4\!:(1)\).  Set
\[
  V_N^\sigma=\frac{\lambda}{4}\,:\!(X_N^\sigma)^4\!:(1),
  \qquad
  V=\frac{\lambda}{4}\,:\!X^4\!:(1).
\]
Then \(V_N^A\to V\) and \(V_N^B\to V\) in \(L^2(\Omega)\).  Consequently,
if the exponential weights \(e^{-V_N^\sigma}\) obey a uniform \(L^p\) bound
for some \(p>1\) and if the cylinder coordinates are finite Wick-coordinate
vectors satisfying
Remark~\ref{rem:spde-common-wick-coordinate-limits}, then the
bounded-cylinder comparison conclusion of
Corollary~\ref{cor:spde-bounded-cylinder-common-potential} applies.
The first statement is Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-two-dimensional-wick-powers}
with \(n=4\) and with the smooth test function \(f\equiv1\).  Multiplication
by the scalar \(\lambda/4\) preserves \(L^2\)-convergence.  The
two cutoff approximations are handled separately by the same Wick-power
convergence proposition; because both sequences have the same \(L^2\) limit,
\[
  \|V_N^\sigma-V\|_{L^2(\Omega)}\longrightarrow0,
  \qquad \sigma=A,B.
\]
The last assertion follows from
Corollary~\ref{cor:spde-bounded-cylinder-common-potential}, since
\(L^2\)-convergence implies convergence in probability of the potentials.
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[Path-space convergence of the two-dimensional enhanced noise]
\label{prop:spde-two-dimensional-enhanced-noise-path-convergence}
Let
\[
  W_N^{(n)}(\tau)=:\!X_N(\tau)^n\!:,
  \qquad n=1,2,3 .
\]
For every \(T<\infty\), every \(s>0\), every
\(0<\vartheta<\min\{s,1\}\), and every integer \(p\geq1\) with
\(p\vartheta>1\), there is a constant
\(C=C(T,s,\vartheta,p,n,m)<\infty\), independent of \(N\), such that
\begin{equation}
  \mathbb E\,
  \|W_N^{(n)}(\tau)-W_N^{(n)}(\sigma)\|_{H^{-s}}^{2p}
  \leq
  C|\tau-\sigma|^{p\vartheta}
  \qquad
  (0\leq\sigma,\tau\leq T).
  \label{eq:spde-two-dimensional-wick-path-increment-bound}
\end{equation}
Consequently the triple
\[
  \mathbb X_N=(X_N,:\!X_N^2\!:,:\!X_N^3\!:)
\]
converges in probability in
\[
  C([0,T];H^{-s}(\mathbb T^2))^3
\]
to a continuous enhanced noise
\(\mathbb X=(X,:\!X^2\!:,:\!X^3\!:)\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Write \(a_k=|k|^2+m^2\).  The stationary Ornstein--Uhlenbeck formula gives,
for \(\tau,\sigma\in\R\),
\begin{equation}
  \mathbb E\!\left[
    X_k(\tau)\overline{X_\ell(\sigma)}
  \right]
  =
  \delta_{k\ell}\frac{e^{-a_k|\tau-\sigma|}}{a_k}.
  \label{eq:spde-ou-time-cross-covariance}
\end{equation}
Indeed, with \(\tau\geq\sigma\),
\[
  X_k(\tau)
  =
  e^{-a_k(\tau-\sigma)}X_k(\sigma)
  +
  \sqrt2\int_{\sigma}^{\tau}e^{-a_k(\tau-r)}\,\dd B_k(r),
\]
and the stochastic integral is independent of \(X_k(\sigma)\).

Let \(\widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\tau)\) denote the \(q\)-th Fourier
coefficient of \(W_N^{(n)}(\tau)\).  Wick's theorem and
\eqref{eq:spde-ou-time-cross-covariance} give
\begin{equation}
  \mathbb E\!\left[
    \widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\tau)
    \overline{\widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\sigma)}
  \right]
  =
  n!\!\!
  \sum_{\substack{k_1+\cdots+k_n=q\\ |k_i|\leq N}}
  \prod_{i=1}^{n}
  \frac{e^{-a_{k_i}|\tau-\sigma|}}{a_{k_i}} .
  \label{eq:spde-wick-time-cross-covariance}
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{align}
  &\mathbb E\left|
    \widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\tau)
    -
    \widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\sigma)
  \right|^2
  \notag\\
  &\qquad =
  2n!\!\!
  \sum_{\substack{k_1+\cdots+k_n=q\\ |k_i|\leq N}}
  \prod_{i=1}^{n}\frac1{a_{k_i}}
  \left(
    1-e^{-|\tau-\sigma|\sum_i a_{k_i}}
  \right).
  \label{eq:spde-wick-time-increment-variance-exact}
\end{align}
For \(0<\vartheta<1\), \(1-e^{-r}\leq r^\vartheta\), and concavity gives
\((\sum_i a_{k_i})^\vartheta\leq\sum_i a_{k_i}^{\vartheta}\).  Hence the
right side of
\eqref{eq:spde-wick-time-increment-variance-exact} is bounded by
\[
  C_{n,\vartheta}|\tau-\sigma|^\vartheta
  \sum_{j=1}^n
  \sum_{k_1+\cdots+k_n=q}
  a_{k_j}^{-1+\vartheta}
  \prod_{i\neq j}a_{k_i}^{-1}.
\]
Let \(g_\vartheta(k)=(1+|k|^2)^{-1+\vartheta}\).  Repeating the
three-region proof of
Lemma~\ref{lem:spde-massive-convolution-bound}, with the factor
\((1+|p|^2)^{-1}\) replaced once by
\((1+|p|^2)^{-1+\vartheta}\), gives
\begin{equation}
  (g_\vartheta*g^{*(n-1)})(q)
  \leq
  C_{n,\vartheta}\,
  \frac{L(q)^n}{(1+|q|^2)^{1-\vartheta}} .
  \label{eq:spde-weakened-propagator-convolution-bound}
\end{equation}
The only changed part of the induction is the region in which the weakened
factor is the small momentum: there
\(\sum_{|p|\leq |q|/2}(1+|p|^2)^{-1+\vartheta}\leq
C_\vartheta(1+|q|^2)^\vartheta\), which changes
\((1+|q|^2)^{-1}\) into \((1+|q|^2)^{-1+\vartheta}\).  All other regions
are bounded by the same or a smaller power, up to logarithms.  Thus
\begin{equation}
  \mathbb E\left|
    \widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\tau)
    -
    \widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\sigma)
  \right|^2
  \leq
  C|\tau-\sigma|^\vartheta
  \frac{L(q)^n}{(1+|q|^2)^{1-\vartheta}} .
  \label{eq:spde-wick-time-increment-fourier-bound}
\end{equation}
After multiplying by the \(H^{-s}\) weight and summing over \(q\), the
large-shell exponent is
\[
  1-2s-2(1-\vartheta)=-1-2s+2\vartheta .
\]
This is strictly smaller than \(-1\) because \(\vartheta<s\).  Hence
\[
  \mathbb E\,
  \|W_N^{(n)}(\tau)-W_N^{(n)}(\sigma)\|_{H^{-s}}^{2}
  \leq
  C|\tau-\sigma|^\vartheta .
\]

Each scalar Fourier coefficient of the increment lies in the \(n\)-th
homogeneous Wiener chaos.  Applying
Lemma~\ref{lem:spde-finite-wiener-chaos-moment-bound} to scalar Fourier
coordinates and using Minkowski's inequality in
\(L^p(\Omega)\) for the series defining the squared \(H^{-s}\)-norm gives
\[
  \mathbb E\,
  \|W_N^{(n)}(\tau)-W_N^{(n)}(\sigma)\|_{H^{-s}}^{2p}
  \leq
  C_{p,n}
  \left(
    \sum_q(1+|q|^2)^{-s}
    \mathbb E\left|
      \widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\tau)
      -
      \widehat W_{N,q}^{(n)}(\sigma)
    \right|^2
  \right)^p ,
\]
which proves \eqref{eq:spde-two-dimensional-wick-path-increment-bound}.

It remains to pass from equal-time convergence to path convergence.  Fix
\(\epsilon,\eta>0\).  By
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-two-dimensional-wick-powers}, for every finite
time grid \(\mathcal G\subset[0,T]\),
\[
  \max_{\tau\in\mathcal G}
  \|W_N^{(n)}(\tau)-W_M^{(n)}(\tau)\|_{H^{-s}}
  \longrightarrow0
  \quad\hbox{in probability as }N,M\to\infty .
\]
The uniform moment bound
\eqref{eq:spde-two-dimensional-wick-path-increment-bound} and the
Kolmogorov dyadic argument imply that, after choosing the mesh of
\(\mathcal G\) sufficiently small,
\[
  \sup_N
  \mathbb P\!\left(
    \sup_{|\tau-\sigma|\leq\operatorname{mesh}(\mathcal G)}
    \|W_N^{(n)}(\tau)-W_N^{(n)}(\sigma)\|_{H^{-s}}
    >\epsilon
  \right)
  <\eta .
\]
The same estimate holds with \(M\) in place of \(N\).  The triangle
inequality between a time and the nearest grid point then shows that
\(\{W_N^{(n)}\}_N\) is Cauchy in probability in
\(C([0,T];H^{-s})\).  Its limit has continuous paths because this Banach
space is complete.  The finite-dimensional time marginals agree with the
equal-time Wick-power limits, so the limiting process is precisely
\(:\!X^n\!:\).  Applying the argument to \(n=1,2,3\) proves the convergence
of \(\mathbb X_N\).
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Heat-kernel smoothing estimate.}
Let \(A=\Delta-m^2\) on \(\mathbb T^d\).  The parabolic gain used below is the
following Fourier estimate: for \(r\in\R\), \(\theta\geq0\), and \(t>0\),
\begin{equation}
  \|e^{tA}u\|_{H^{r+2\theta}}
  \leq
  C_\theta t^{-\theta}e^{-m^2t/2}\|u\|_{H^r}.
  \label{eq:spde-heat-kernel-smoothing}
\end{equation}
Consequently, if \(F\in L^\infty([0,T];H^r)\), then
\begin{equation}
  \left\|
    \int_0^t e^{(t-s)A}F(s)\,\dd s
  \right\|_{H^{r+2\theta}}
  \leq
  C_{\theta,T}\|F\|_{L^\infty([0,T];H^r)}
  \quad(0\leq\theta<1).
  \label{eq:spde-duhamel-smoothing}
\end{equation}
Indeed, in Fourier coordinates,
\[
  \|e^{tA}u\|_{H^{r+2\theta}}^2
  =
  \sum_k
  (1+|k|^2)^{r+2\theta}
  e^{-2t(|k|^2+m^2)}
  |\widehat u(k)|^2 .
\]
The elementary bound
\[
  (1+|k|^2)^{2\theta}e^{-t(|k|^2+m^2)}
  \leq C_\theta t^{-2\theta}e^{-m^2t/2}
\]
gives \eqref{eq:spde-heat-kernel-smoothing}.  The Duhamel estimate follows
by integrating
\((t-s)^{-\theta}\) over \(0<s<t\), which is finite exactly for
\(\theta<1\).
The restriction \(\theta<1\) is therefore not a stochastic effect; it is the
endpoint singularity of the deterministic heat kernel in this \(L^\infty_t\)
estimate.  Sharper parabolic Besov--Holder versions distribute time
regularity differently, but the fixed-point argument below only needs the
displayed Sobolev form.

\section{Da Prato--Debussche in Two Dimensions}

On a two-dimensional torus, consider the stochastic quantization equation
for the Wick-ordered measure
\begin{equation}
  \partial_\tau\Phi
  =
  \Delta\Phi-m^2\Phi-\lambda:\!\Phi^3\!:+\xi .
  \label{eq:dpd-phi-four-two}
\end{equation}
Let \(X\) solve the stationary linear equation
\begin{equation}
  \partial_\tau X=(\Delta-m^2)X+\xi .
  \label{eq:ornstein-uhlenbeck-field}
\end{equation}
The field \(X(\tau,\cdot)\) has the massive Gaussian free-field law on the
spatial torus and is almost surely a spatial distribution of regularity
\(-\kappa\) for every \(\kappa>0\).  Write
\[
  \Phi=X+Y.
\]
Then the nonlinear equation for \(Y\) is
\begin{equation}
  \partial_\tau Y
  =
  (\Delta-m^2)Y
  -\lambda\bigl(
    Y^3+3Y^2X+3Y:\!X^2\!:+:\!X^3\!:
  \bigr).
  \label{eq:dpd-remainder-equation}
\end{equation}
In two dimensions the products in
\eqref{eq:dpd-remainder-equation} are well-defined once
\(:\!X^2\!:\) and \(:\!X^3\!:\) are constructed as Wick powers, because the
unknown \(Y\) gains positive parabolic regularity from the heat operator.

\subsection{Local Product Estimates and Mild Fixed Points}
\label{subsec:spde-dpd-local-product-fixed-point}

\begin{lemma}[Sobolev products for the DPD fixed point]
\label{lem:spde-dpd-sobolev-products}
Let \(0<\kappa<1/4\) and set \(\beta=1+2\kappa\).  On \(\mathbb T^2\),
there is \(C_\kappa<\infty\) such that
\begin{align}
  \|uv\|_{H^\beta}
  &\leq C_\kappa\|u\|_{H^\beta}\|v\|_{H^\beta},
  \label{eq:spde-hbeta-algebra}\\
  \|uv\|_{H^{-\kappa}}
  &\leq C_\kappa\|u\|_{H^\beta}\|v\|_{H^{-\kappa}},
  \label{eq:spde-hbeta-negative-multiplier}
\end{align}
for \(u,v\) in the indicated spaces.  Consequently, if
\(\mathbb X=(X,X^{(2)},X^{(3)})\in C([0,T];H^{-\kappa})^3\) and
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal N(Y,\mathbb X)
  =
  Y^3+3Y^2X+3YX^{(2)}+X^{(3)},
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-nonlinearity}
\end{equation}
then \(\mathcal N(Y,\mathbb X)\in C([0,T];H^{-\kappa})\) for every
\(Y\in C([0,T];H^\beta)\).  We write
\[
  \|\mathbb X\|_{C_TH^{-\kappa}}
  =
  \max_{1\leq j\leq3}\|X^{(j)}\|_{C([0,T];H^{-\kappa})},
  \qquad X^{(1)}=X,
\]
and for
\(\|Y\|_{C_TH^\beta},\|Z\|_{C_TH^\beta}\leq R\),
\begin{equation}
  \|\mathcal N(Y,\mathbb X)-\mathcal N(Z,\mathbb X)\|_{C_TH^{-\kappa}}
  \leq
  C_{\kappa,R}\bigl(1+\|\mathbb X\|_{C_TH^{-\kappa}}\bigr)
  \|Y-Z\|_{C_TH^\beta}.
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-nonlinearity-lipschitz}
\end{equation}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
We recall the dyadic proof because this is the exact multiplication input in
the fixed point.  Let \(\Delta_j\) be a Littlewood--Paley partition on the
torus and \(S_j=\sum_{i<j}\Delta_i\).  Bony's decomposition writes
\[
  uv=u\prec v+u\circ v+u\succ v,
  \qquad
  u\prec v=\sum_j S_{j-1}u\,\Delta_jv ,
\]
with \(u\circ v=\sum_{|i-j|\leq1}\Delta_i u\,\Delta_jv\).  Bernstein's
inequality in two dimensions gives
\begin{equation}
  \|\Delta_j u\|_{L^\infty}\leq C2^{j(1-\beta)}\|u\|_{H^\beta}
  \qquad(\beta>1),
  \label{eq:spde-bernstein-hbeta-linfty-block}
\end{equation}
and summing in \(j\) gives \(H^\beta\hookrightarrow L^\infty\).  The
paraproduct estimates following from the almost-disjoint frequency supports
are
\begin{equation}
  \|u\prec v\|_{H^s}+\|u\succ v\|_{H^s}
  \leq C\|u\|_{L^\infty}\|v\|_{H^s}
       +C\|v\|_{L^\infty}\|u\|_{H^s},
\end{equation}
and, for the resonant term,
\begin{equation}
  \|u\circ v\|_{H^{s+t-1}}
  \leq C\|u\|_{H^s}\|v\|_{H^t}
  \quad\text{when }s+t>1 .
  \label{eq:spde-resonant-product-estimate}
\end{equation}
Indeed, \(\Delta_\ell(u\prec v)\) only receives terms with
\(|j-\ell|\leq O(1)\), so
\[
  2^{\ell s}\|\Delta_\ell(u\prec v)\|_{L^2}
  \leq
  C\|u\|_{L^\infty}
  \sum_{|j-\ell|\leq O(1)}2^{js}\|\Delta_jv\|_{L^2},
\]
and square summation in \(\ell\) gives the first paraproduct bound; the
bound for \(u\succ v\) is identical.  For \(u\circ v\), the product
\(\Delta_i u\,\Delta_j v\) with \(|i-j|\leq1\) has frequencies
\(\lesssim 2^j\).  Bernstein and Cauchy--Schwarz give
\(\|\Delta_i u\,\Delta_jv\|_{L^2}\leq
C2^{j(1-s-t)}a_ib_j\), where
\(a_j=2^{js}\|\Delta_j u\|_{L^2}\) and
\(b_j=2^{jt}\|\Delta_jv\|_{L^2}\).  Multiplication by the
\(H^{s+t-1}\) weight cancels the factor \(2^{j(1-s-t)}\), and the condition
\(s+t>1\) makes the finite-overlap high-frequency convolution summable.
Taking \(s=t=\beta\) gives the algebra estimate
\eqref{eq:spde-hbeta-algebra}, since \(2\beta-1>\beta\).  Taking
\(s=\beta\) and \(t=-\kappa\) gives
\(\beta-\kappa>1\), so the resonant term lands in
\(H^{\beta-\kappa-1}\).  Because \(\beta-\kappa-1=\kappa>-\kappa\), this
term is in \(H^{-\kappa}\), and the two paraproduct terms are controlled by
the \(L^\infty\) embedding of \(H^\beta\).  This proves
\eqref{eq:spde-hbeta-negative-multiplier}.

The bounds on \(\mathcal N\) follow by applying
\eqref{eq:spde-hbeta-algebra} and
\eqref{eq:spde-hbeta-negative-multiplier} term by term:
\[
  \|Y^3\|_{H^\beta}\leq C\|Y\|_{H^\beta}^3,
  \quad
  \|Y^2X\|_{H^{-\kappa}}\leq C\|Y\|_{H^\beta}^2\|X\|_{H^{-\kappa}},
\]
and similarly for \(YX^{(2)}\).  For the Lipschitz estimate, use
\[
  Y^3-Z^3=(Y-Z)(Y^2+YZ+Z^2),
  \qquad
  Y^2X-Z^2X=(Y-Z)(Y+Z)X,
\]
and the same two product estimates.  This gives
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-nonlinearity-lipschitz}.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Besov--Holder product continuity for the DPD nonlinearity]
\label{prop:spde-dpd-besov-product-continuity}
Let \(0<\kappa<\alpha\), and let
\[
  \mathcal C^\gamma(\mathbb T^2)=B^\gamma_{\infty,\infty}(\mathbb T^2),
  \qquad
  \|u\|_{\mathcal C^\gamma}
  =
  \sup_{j\geq -1}2^{j\gamma}\|\Delta_j u\|_{L^\infty}.
\]
Here \((\Delta_j)_{j\geq -1}\) is a fixed smooth dyadic partition of unity
on the torus, with \(\Delta_{-1}\) the low-frequency block.  Then Bony's
formula defines canonical continuous bilinear maps extending ordinary
multiplication of smooth functions,
\[
  \mathcal C^\alpha\times\mathcal C^{-\kappa}
  \longrightarrow
  \mathcal C^{-\kappa},
  \qquad
  \mathcal C^\alpha\times\mathcal C^\alpha
  \longrightarrow
  \mathcal C^\alpha .
\]
Consequently, for
\[
  \mathbb X=(X_1,X_2,X_3)\in C([0,T];\mathcal C^{-\kappa})^3
  \quad\text{and}\quad
  Y\in C([0,T];\mathcal C^\alpha),
\]
the expression
\[
  \mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Y,\mathbb X)
  =
  Y^3+3Y^2X_1+3YX_2+X_3
\]
is a well-defined element of
\(C([0,T];\mathcal C^{-\kappa})\).  Here
\[
  \|\mathbb X\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  =
  \max_{1\leq i\leq3}
  \|X_i\|_{C([0,T];\mathcal C^{-\kappa})}.
\]
The same convention is used for any other triple
\(\mathbb Z=(Z_1,Z_2,Z_3)\).
More precisely, for every \(R>0\)
there is \(C_{\alpha,\kappa,R}\) such that, whenever
\(\mathbb Z=(Z_1,Z_2,Z_3)\) and
\[
  \|Y\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}
  +\|Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}
  +\|\mathbb X\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  +\|\mathbb Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  \leq R ,
\]
one has
\begin{align}
  &\|\mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Y,\mathbb X)
      -\mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Z,\mathbb Z)\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  \notag\\
  &\qquad\leq
  C_{\alpha,\kappa,R}
  \left(
    \|Y-Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}
    +
    \max_{1\leq i\leq3}
    \|X_i-Z_i\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  \right).
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-besov-nonlinearity-continuity}
\end{align}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
It is enough to prove the two multiplication statements, since the
continuity of \(\mathcal N_{\mathcal C}\) then follows by expanding
differences:
\[
  Y^3-Z^3=(Y-Z)(Y^2+YZ+Z^2),
  \qquad
  Y^2X_1-Z^2Z_1=(Y^2-Z^2)X_1+Z^2(X_1-Z_1),
\]
and similarly for \(YX_2-ZZ_2\).

Use Bony's decomposition
\[
  fg=f\prec g+f\circ g+f\succ g,
  \qquad
  f\prec g=\sum_j S_{j-1}f\,\Delta_jg ,
\]
where \(S_j=\sum_{i<j}\Delta_i\) and
\[
  f\circ g=\sum_{|i-j|\leq1}\Delta_if\,\Delta_jg .
\]
The frequency supports imply that \(\Delta_\ell(f\prec g)\) receives only
\(|j-\ell|\leq C_0\).  Hence, for any \(\beta\in\mathbb R\),
\[
  2^{\ell\beta}\|\Delta_\ell(f\prec g)\|_{L^\infty}
  \leq
  C\|f\|_{L^\infty}
  \sup_{|j-\ell|\leq C_0}
  2^{j\beta}\|\Delta_jg\|_{L^\infty},
\]
and therefore
\begin{equation}
  \|f\prec g\|_{\mathcal C^\beta}
  \leq
  C\|f\|_{L^\infty}\|g\|_{\mathcal C^\beta}.
  \label{eq:spde-besov-left-paraproduct}
\end{equation}
If \(\beta<0\), then the low-frequency sum satisfies
\[
  \|S_{j-1}g\|_{L^\infty}
  \leq
  C2^{-j\beta}\|g\|_{\mathcal C^\beta},
\]
because \(\sum_{i<j}2^{-i\beta}\leq C2^{-j\beta}\).  Thus, for
\(\alpha>0\) and \(\beta<0\),
\begin{equation}
  \|g\prec f\|_{\mathcal C^{\alpha+\beta}}
  \leq
  C\|g\|_{\mathcal C^\beta}\|f\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}.
  \label{eq:spde-besov-right-paraproduct}
\end{equation}
For the resonant term, the block \(\Delta_\ell(f\circ g)\) receives only
indices \(i,j\geq \ell-C_1\) with \(|i-j|\leq1\).  Therefore
\[
\begin{aligned}
  2^{\ell(\alpha+\beta)}
  \|\Delta_\ell(f\circ g)\|_{L^\infty}
  &\leq
  C\|f\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}\|g\|_{\mathcal C^\beta}
  \sum_{i\geq \ell-C_1}2^{(\ell-i)(\alpha+\beta)} .
\end{aligned}
\]
The final geometric sum is bounded exactly when \(\alpha+\beta>0\), and
then
\begin{equation}
  \|f\circ g\|_{\mathcal C^{\alpha+\beta}}
  \leq
  C\|f\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}\|g\|_{\mathcal C^\beta}.
  \label{eq:spde-besov-resonant-product}
\end{equation}

Now take \(\beta=-\kappa\).  Since \(\alpha-\kappa>0\),
\eqref{eq:spde-besov-left-paraproduct},
\eqref{eq:spde-besov-right-paraproduct}, and
\eqref{eq:spde-besov-resonant-product} give
\[
  \|fg\|_{\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  \leq
  C_{\alpha,\kappa}
  \|f\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}\|g\|_{\mathcal C^{-\kappa}},
\]
using the continuous embedding
\(\mathcal C^{\alpha-\kappa}\hookrightarrow\mathcal C^{-\kappa}\).
Taking instead \(f,g\in\mathcal C^\alpha\), the paraproduct terms are
controlled by \(\mathcal C^\alpha\hookrightarrow L^\infty\), and the
resonant term belongs to \(\mathcal C^{2\alpha}\), hence to
\(\mathcal C^\alpha\).  This proves that
\(\mathcal C^\alpha\) is an algebra.  The bilinear maps are therefore
well-defined by the displayed paraproduct and resonant sums.  On smooth
inputs the dyadic partition is a partition of unity, so Bony's formula
equals the ordinary pointwise product; on rough inputs the formula is the
canonical product used in this Besov scale.  If a sequence of smooth inputs
converges in a slightly stronger Besov norm, the bilinear estimates show
that its ordinary products converge to the same paraproduct value.

Applying these two bilinear estimates at each time \(t\), taking suprema in
time, and expanding the displayed differences gives
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-nonlinearity-continuity}.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Besov heat smoothing on the torus]
\label{prop:spde-besov-heat-smoothing}
Let \(m^2>0\), \(0<\theta<1\), and
\(A=\Delta-m^2\) on \(\mathbb T^2\).  For every \(\rho\in\mathbb R\)
there is \(C_{\rho,\theta,m}<\infty\) such that
\begin{equation}
  \|e^{tA}f\|_{\mathcal C^{\rho+2\theta}}
  \leq
  C_{\rho,\theta,m}\,
  t^{-\theta}\,
  \|f\|_{\mathcal C^\rho},
  \qquad 0<t\leq1.
  \label{eq:spde-besov-heat-smoothing}
\end{equation}
Consequently, if \(F\in C([0,T];\mathcal C^\rho)\) and \(T\leq1\), then
\[
  \left\|
    \int_0^t e^{(t-s)A}F(s)\,\dd s
  \right\|_{C([0,T];\mathcal C^{\rho+2\theta})}
  \leq
  C_{\rho,\theta,m}\,
  T^{1-\theta}
  \|F\|_{C([0,T];\mathcal C^\rho)} .
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Let \((\Delta_j)_{j\geq-1}\) be the dyadic partition used in
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-besov-product-continuity}.  For
\(j\geq0\), let \(K_{j,t}\) be the convolution kernel on \(\mathbb T^2\)
of the multiplier
\[
  k\longmapsto
  \varphi(2^{-j}k)\exp(-t(|k|^2+m^2)),
\]
where \(\varphi\) is the annular cutoff defining \(\Delta_j\).  The
rescaled multiplier
\[
  \xi\longmapsto
  \varphi(\xi)\exp(-t(2^{2j}|\xi|^2+m^2))
\]
has derivatives of every fixed order bounded by
\[
  C_N(1+t2^{2j})^N\exp(-c\,t2^{2j}),
\]
hence by \(C_N\exp(-c_Nt2^{2j})\).  The standard
integration-by-parts estimate for Fourier kernels with compactly supported
smooth multipliers, applied after this rescaling and then periodized to
\(\mathbb T^2\), gives
\[
  \|K_{j,t}\|_{L^1(\mathbb T^2)}
  \leq
  C_N(1+t2^{2j})^{-N}
  \leq
  C_\theta t^{-\theta}2^{-2\theta j},
  \qquad j\geq0,\quad 0<t\leq1,
\]
after choosing \(N>\theta\).  The low block \(\Delta_{-1}\) has a smooth
compactly supported multiplier, so its kernel has uniformly bounded
\(L^1\)-norm and is absorbed by increasing the constant.  Since
\(\Delta_j\) commutes with \(e^{tA}\), Young's inequality gives
\[
\begin{aligned}
  2^{j(\rho+2\theta)}
  \|\Delta_j e^{tA}f\|_{L^\infty}
  &\leq
  C_{\theta,m}t^{-\theta}
  2^{j\rho}\|\Delta_jf\|_{L^\infty}.
\end{aligned}
\]
Taking the supremum over \(j\geq-1\) proves
\eqref{eq:spde-besov-heat-smoothing}.

For the Duhamel term, apply
\eqref{eq:spde-besov-heat-smoothing} to \(e^{(t-s)A}F(s)\) and integrate:
\[
  \left\|
    \int_0^t e^{(t-s)A}F(s)\,\dd s
  \right\|_{\mathcal C^{\rho+2\theta}}
  \leq
  C_{\rho,\theta,m}
  \|F\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\rho}
  \int_0^t(t-s)^{-\theta}\,\dd s .
\]
Since \(\theta<1\), the last integral is
\(t^{1-\theta}/(1-\theta)\), uniformly bounded by a constant times
\(T^{1-\theta}\).
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Deterministic DPD local fixed point in Besov--Holder coordinates]
\label{thm:spde-dpd-besov-local-fixed-point}
Let \(m^2>0\), \(\lambda>0\), and choose exponents
\[
  0<\kappa<\alpha<2-\kappa .
\]
Let
\[
  \mathbb X=(X_1,X_2,X_3)
  \in C([0,T_0];\mathcal C^{-\kappa}(\mathbb T^2))^3 ,
  \qquad
  Y_0\in\mathcal C^\alpha(\mathbb T^2).
\]
Set
\[
  M_{\mathbb X}
  =
  \max_{1\leq i\leq3}\|X_i\|_{C([0,T_0];\mathcal C^{-\kappa})}.
\]
For another enhanced noise
\(\mathbb Z=(Z_1,Z_2,Z_3)\), the notation
\(\|\mathbb Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}\) below always means the same
maximum over the three components restricted to \([0,T]\).
Then there is \(T\in(0,T_0]\), depending only on
\[
  \alpha,\kappa,m,\lambda,\|Y_0\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha},
  \quad\text{and}\quad M_{\mathbb X},
\]
such that the mild equation
\begin{equation}
  Y(t)
  =
  e^{t(\Delta-m^2)}Y_0
  -
  \lambda
  \int_0^t
  e^{(t-s)(\Delta-m^2)}
  \mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Y(s),\mathbb X(s))\,\dd s
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-besov-mild-equation}
\end{equation}
has a unique solution \(Y\in C([0,T];\mathcal C^\alpha)\).  On every ball
where
\[
  \|Y_0\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}
  +\|Z_0\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}
  +\|\mathbb X\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  +\|\mathbb Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  \leq R ,
\]
and for \(T\) chosen depending only on
\(\alpha,\kappa,m,\lambda,R\), the solution map
\[
  (Y_0,\mathbb X)\longmapsto Y
\]
is Lipschitz from
\[
  \mathcal C^\alpha\times
  \bigl(C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}\bigr)^3
\]
to
\(C_T\mathcal C^\alpha\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Put
\[
  \theta=\frac{\alpha+\kappa}{2}.
\]
The hypothesis \(\alpha<2-\kappa\) is exactly \(\theta<1\), which is the
integrability condition for the Duhamel kernel.  Let \(A=\Delta-m^2\) and
define, for \(Y\in C_T\mathcal C^\alpha\),
\[
  (\Psi Y)(t)
  =
  e^{tA}Y_0
  -
  \lambda
  \int_0^t
  e^{(t-s)A}\mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Y(s),\mathbb X(s))\,\dd s .
\]
By Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-besov-product-continuity},
\(\mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Y,\mathbb X)\in C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}\).
Applying Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-besov-heat-smoothing} with
\(\rho=-\kappa\) and \(2\theta=\alpha+\kappa\) gives
\begin{equation}
  \left\|
    \int_0^t
    e^{(t-s)A}
    \mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Y(s),\mathbb X(s))\,\dd s
  \right\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}
  \leq
  C T^{1-\theta}
  \|\mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Y,\mathbb X)\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}} .
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-besov-duhamel-gain}
\end{equation}
The heat semigroup is bounded on \(\mathcal C^\alpha\).  Choose
\[
  R_0=2C\|Y_0\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}+1 .
\]
For \(\|Y\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}\leq R_0\), the product-continuity
proposition gives
\[
  \|\mathcal N_{\mathcal C}(Y,\mathbb X)\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  \leq
  C_{\alpha,\kappa,R_0+M_{\mathbb X}} .
\]
Taking \(T\leq T_0\) so that the right side of
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-duhamel-gain}, multiplied by \(\lambda\), is at
most \(R_0/2\), the map \(\Psi\) sends the closed ball
\[
  B_{R_0}=\{Y\in C_T\mathcal C^\alpha:
    \|Y\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}\leq R_0\}
\]
to itself.

For \(Y,Z\in B_{R_0}\), equations
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-nonlinearity-continuity} and
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-duhamel-gain} give
\[
  \|\Psi Y-\Psi Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}
  \leq
  \lambda C_{\alpha,\kappa,m,R_0+M_{\mathbb X}}
  T^{1-\theta}
  \|Y-Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}.
\]
Decreasing \(T\) if necessary makes the coefficient strictly smaller than
one.  Since \(C_T\mathcal C^\alpha\) is complete, Banach's fixed-point
theorem gives a unique fixed point in \(B_{R_0}\).  If another solution
exists on the same interval with finite \(C_T\mathcal C^\alpha\) norm, the
same contraction argument on a smaller ball containing both solutions,
possibly after partitioning the time interval into finitely many subintervals,
forces equality.

For Lipschitz dependence, let \(Y\) and \(Z\) solve
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-mild-equation} with data
\((Y_0,\mathbb X)\) and \((Z_0,\mathbb Z)\), all bounded by \(R\).  The
semigroup bound, the Duhamel estimate, and
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-nonlinearity-continuity} give
\[
\begin{aligned}
  \|Y-Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}
  &\leq
  C\|Y_0-Z_0\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}
\\
  &\quad+
  \lambda C_{\alpha,\kappa,m,R}
  T^{1-\theta}
  \left(
    \|Y-Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}
    +
    \max_i\|X_i-Z_i\|_{C_T\mathcal C^{-\kappa}}
  \right).
\end{aligned}
\]
Choose \(T\) so the coefficient of
\(\|Y-Z\|_{C_T\mathcal C^\alpha}\) on the right is at most \(1/2\), and
absorb it to the left.  This proves the stated Lipschitz bound.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Deterministic DPD local fixed point in Sobolev coordinates]
\label{thm:spde-dpd-deterministic-local-fixed-point}
Let \(0<\kappa<1/4\), \(\beta=1+2\kappa\), \(m^2>0\), \(\lambda>0\), and
\[
  \mathbb X=(X,X^{(2)},X^{(3)})\in C([0,T_0];H^{-\kappa}(\mathbb T^2))^3 .
\]
Set
\[
  M_{\mathbb X}
  =
  \max_{1\leq j\leq3}
  \|X^{(j)}\|_{C([0,T_0];H^{-\kappa})},
  \qquad X^{(1)}=X .
\]
For every \(Y_0\in H^\beta(\mathbb T^2)\) there is
\(T\in(0,T_0]\), depending only on
\(\|Y_0\|_{H^\beta}\), \(\lambda\), \(m\), \(\kappa\), and \(M_{\mathbb X}\),
such that the mild equation
\begin{equation}
  Y(t)
  =
  e^{t(\Delta-m^2)}Y_0
  -
  \lambda\int_0^t
  e^{(t-s)(\Delta-m^2)}
  \mathcal N(Y(s),\mathbb X(s))\,\dd s
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-mild-fixed-point}
\end{equation}
has a unique solution \(Y\in C([0,T];H^\beta)\).  The solution depends
locally Lipschitz continuously on \(Y_0\) and on \(\mathbb X\) in these
norms.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Let \(C_T H^r=C([0,T];H^r)\) and put \(A=\Delta-m^2\).  Define
\[
  (\Psi Y)(t)
  =
  e^{tA}Y_0
  -
  \lambda\int_0^t e^{(t-s)A}\mathcal N(Y(s),\mathbb X(s))\,\dd s .
\]
The forcing belongs to \(C_T H^{-\kappa}\) by
Lemma~\ref{lem:spde-dpd-sobolev-products}.  To estimate the Duhamel term in
\(H^\beta\), use the heat-kernel estimate
\eqref{eq:spde-heat-kernel-smoothing} with
\[
  r=-\kappa,\qquad
  r+2\theta=\beta,\qquad
  \theta=\frac{\beta+\kappa}{2}=\frac{1+3\kappa}{2}.
\]
Since \(\kappa<1/4\), \(\theta<1\).  Hence
\begin{equation}
  \left\|
    \int_0^t e^{(t-s)A}F(s)\,\dd s
  \right\|_{C_TH^\beta}
  \leq
  C_{\kappa,m}T^{1-\theta}\|F\|_{C_TH^{-\kappa}} .
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-duhamel-time-gain}
\end{equation}
Choose \(R=2C\|Y_0\|_{H^\beta}+1\), where \(C\) bounds the heat semigroup
on \(H^\beta\).  Lemma~\ref{lem:spde-dpd-sobolev-products} gives, for
\(\|Y\|_{C_TH^\beta}\leq R\),
\[
  \|\mathcal N(Y,\mathbb X)\|_{C_TH^{-\kappa}}
  \leq
  C_{\kappa,R}\bigl(1+\|\mathbb X\|_{C_TH^{-\kappa}}\bigr).
\]
By \eqref{eq:spde-dpd-duhamel-time-gain}, \(T\) can be chosen so small that
\(\Psi\) maps the closed ball \(B_R\subset C_TH^\beta\) into itself.
For \(Y,Z\in B_R\), equations
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-nonlinearity-lipschitz} and
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-duhamel-time-gain} give
\[
  \|\Psi Y-\Psi Z\|_{C_TH^\beta}
  \leq
  |\lambda|C_{\kappa,R,m}
  T^{1-\theta}
  \bigl(1+\|\mathbb X\|_{C_TH^{-\kappa}}\bigr)
  \|Y-Z\|_{C_TH^\beta}.
\]
Reducing \(T\) once more makes the coefficient \(<1\).  Starting from
any \(Y_0^\sharp\in B_R\) and setting \(Y_{n+1}^\sharp=\Psi Y_n^\sharp\),
the differences obey
\[
  \|Y_{n+1}^\sharp-Y_n^\sharp\|_{C_TH^\beta}
  \leq q^n\|Y_1^\sharp-Y_0^\sharp\|_{C_TH^\beta},
  \qquad q<1.
\]
The resulting sequence is Cauchy in the complete space \(C_TH^\beta\);
its limit is a fixed point, and the same inequality applied to two fixed
points forces equality.  Applying the contraction estimate to two sets of
data gives local Lipschitz dependence, after taking the smaller of the two
local existence times.
\end{proof}

This theorem is deliberately weaker than the sharp Da Prato--Debussche
theorem in Holder--Besov spaces, but it proves the exact deterministic
logic: enhanced noise first makes every term in the nonlinearity meaningful,
then heat smoothing supplies a positive time power, and the local solution
is obtained by contraction.  The remaining global and invariant-measure
steps require estimates beyond this local theorem.

\subsection{Energy Inequalities and Compactness of Smooth Approximations}
\label{subsec:spde-dpd-energy-compactness}

\begin{lemma}[Smooth enhanced-noise energy estimate]
\label{lem:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-estimate}
Let \(X_1,X_2,X_3\) be smooth real functions on
\([0,T]\times\mathbb T^2\), let \(m^2>0\), \(\lambda>0\), and let \(Y\) be
a smooth solution of
\begin{equation}
  \partial_tY
  =
  (\Delta-m^2)Y
  -\lambda\bigl(Y^3+3Y^2X_1+3YX_2+X_3\bigr).
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-equation}
\end{equation}
Then, for a numerical constant \(C\),
\begin{align}
  \frac12\frac{d}{dt}\|Y(t)\|_{L^2}^2
  &+
  \|\nabla Y(t)\|_{L^2}^2
  +m^2\|Y(t)\|_{L^2}^2
  +\frac{\lambda}{4}\|Y(t)\|_{L^4}^4
  \notag\\
  &\leq
  C\lambda
  \left(
    \|X_1(t)\|_{L^4}^4
    +\|X_2(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +\|X_3(t)\|_{L^{4/3}}^{4/3}
  \right).
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-estimate}
\end{align}
Consequently no finite-time blowup can occur in a smooth Galerkin
approximation whose enhanced noise norms on the right-hand side stay
integrable on compact stochastic-time intervals.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Multiply \eqref{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-equation} by \(Y\) and integrate
over the torus.  Periodicity gives
\[
  \int_{\mathbb T^2}Y\Delta Y\,\dd^2x
  =
  -\|\nabla Y\|_{L^2}^2 .
\]
Thus
\[
\begin{aligned}
  \frac12\frac{d}{dt}\|Y\|_{L^2}^2
  &+
  \|\nabla Y\|_{L^2}^2
  +m^2\|Y\|_{L^2}^2
  +\lambda\|Y\|_{L^4}^4 \\
  &=
  -3\lambda\int Y^3X_1
  -3\lambda\int Y^2X_2
  -\lambda\int YX_3 .
\end{aligned}
\]
Young's inequality with conjugate exponents \((4/3,4)\), \((2,2)\), and
\((4,4/3)\) gives pointwise bounds
\[
\begin{aligned}
  3|Y|^3|X_1|
  &\leq \frac14 |Y|^4+C|X_1|^4,\\
  3|Y|^2|X_2|
  &\leq \frac14 |Y|^4+C|X_2|^2,\\
  |Y||X_3|
  &\leq \frac14 |Y|^4+C|X_3|^{4/3}.
\end{aligned}
\]
Substituting these three estimates into the energy identity and retaining
the remaining positive \(\lambda\|Y\|_{L^4}^4/4\) proves
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-estimate}.  Integrating the inequality in
time bounds \(\|Y(t)\|_{L^2}^2\), the accumulated \(H^1\) norm, and the
accumulated \(L^4\) norm by the initial \(L^2\) norm and the three enhanced
noise norms.  A smooth Galerkin solution can be continued as long as its
finite-dimensional norm is finite; the displayed bound prevents blowup on
intervals where the right-hand side is integrable.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Closedness of the DPD energy inequality under approximation]
\label{prop:spde-dpd-energy-closed-under-approximation}
Let \(m^2>0\), \(\lambda>0\), and \(T>0\).  For \(n\geq1\), let
\((X_{1,n},X_{2,n},X_{3,n})\) be smooth real functions on
\([0,T]\times\mathbb T^2\), and let \(Y_n\) be a smooth solution of
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-equation} with \(X_i\) replaced by
\(X_{i,n}\).  Assume
\[
\begin{aligned}
  X_{1,n}&\to X_1
    &&\text{in }L^4([0,T]\times\mathbb T^2),\\
  X_{2,n}&\to X_2
    &&\text{in }L^2([0,T]\times\mathbb T^2),\\
  X_{3,n}&\to X_3
    &&\text{in }L^{4/3}([0,T]\times\mathbb T^2),
\end{aligned}
\]
and suppose that \(Y_n(0)\to Y_0\) strongly in \(L^2(\mathbb T^2)\).  Let
\(Y\) be a function such that, after passing to a subsequence,
\[
\begin{aligned}
  Y_n&\rightharpoonup Y
    &&\text{weakly in }L^2([0,T];H^1(\mathbb T^2)),\\
  Y_n&\rightharpoonup Y
    &&\text{weakly in }L^4([0,T]\times\mathbb T^2),\\
  Y_n(T)&\rightharpoonup Y(T)
    &&\text{weakly in }L^2(\mathbb T^2).
\end{aligned}
\]
Then \(Y\) satisfies the integrated energy inequality
\begin{align}
  \frac12\|Y(T)\|_{L^2}^2
  &+
  \int_0^T
  \left[
    \|\nabla Y(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +m^2\|Y(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +\frac{\lambda}{4}\|Y(t)\|_{L^4}^4
  \right]\dd t
  \notag\\
  &\leq
  \frac12\|Y_0\|_{L^2}^2
  +
  C\lambda
  \int_0^T
  \left[
    \|X_1(t)\|_{L^4}^4
    +\|X_2(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +\|X_3(t)\|_{L^{4/3}}^{4/3}
  \right]\dd t .
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-closed-energy-inequality}
\end{align}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Integrating
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-estimate} for the \(n\)-th smooth solution
gives
\begin{align}
  \frac12\|Y_n(T)\|_{L^2}^2
  &+
  \int_0^T
  \left[
    \|\nabla Y_n(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +m^2\|Y_n(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +\frac{\lambda}{4}\|Y_n(t)\|_{L^4}^4
  \right]\dd t
  \notag\\
  &\leq
  \frac12\|Y_n(0)\|_{L^2}^2
  +
  C\lambda
  \int_0^T
  \left[
    \|X_{1,n}(t)\|_{L^4}^4
    +\|X_{2,n}(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +\|X_{3,n}(t)\|_{L^{4/3}}^{4/3}
  \right]\dd t .
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-integrated-energy-n}
\end{align}
The right hand side converges to the right hand side of
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-closed-energy-inequality}: the initial data converge
strongly in \(L^2\), and convergence in \(L^p\) implies convergence of
\(\|X_{1,n}\|_{L^4}^4\), \(\|X_{2,n}\|_{L^2}^2\), and
\(\|X_{3,n}\|_{L^{4/3}}^{4/3}\) by the elementary inequality
\[
  \bigl||a|^p-|b|^p\bigr|
  \leq
  p\,|a-b|(|a|+|b|)^{p-1},
  \qquad p\geq1,
\]
followed by Hölder's inequality.

The left hand side is lower semicontinuous under the assumed weak
convergences.  For the quadratic terms this is the lower semicontinuity of
the norm in Hilbert space, applied in
\[
  L^2([0,T];H^1(\mathbb T^2)),
  \qquad
  L^2([0,T];L^2(\mathbb T^2)),
\]
and also at the endpoint \(T\).  For the quartic term,
the map
\[
  u\longmapsto
  \|u\|_{L^4([0,T]\times\mathbb T^2)}^4
\]
is convex and norm-continuous on \(L^4\), hence weakly lower
semicontinuous.  Taking the liminf of the left hand side of
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-integrated-energy-n} and the limit of its right
hand side proves \eqref{eq:spde-dpd-closed-energy-inequality}.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Energy compactness for smooth DPD remainders]
\label{prop:spde-dpd-energy-compactness}
Let \(m^2>0\), \(\lambda>0\), \(T>0\), and set
\(Q_T=[0,T]\times\mathbb T^2\).  For \(n\geq1\), let
\((X_{1,n},X_{2,n},X_{3,n})\) be smooth real functions on \(Q_T\), and let
\(Y_n\) be a smooth solution of
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-equation} with \(X_i\) replaced by
\(X_{i,n}\).  Assume that
\[
  M_T:=
  \sup_n
  \left[
    \|Y_n(0)\|_{L^2(\mathbb T^2)}
    +\|X_{1,n}\|_{L^4(Q_T)}
    +\|X_{2,n}\|_{L^2(Q_T)}
    +\|X_{3,n}\|_{L^{4/3}(Q_T)}
  \right]
  <\infty .
\]
Then there is a constant \(C_T\), depending on
\(T,m^2,\lambda\), and \(M_T\), but not on \(n\), such that
\begin{align}
  \sup_n
  \Big[
    &\|Y_n\|_{L^\infty([0,T];L^2)}
     +\|Y_n\|_{L^2([0,T];H^1)}
     +\|Y_n\|_{L^4(Q_T)}
  \notag\\
    &\hspace{2.5cm}
     +\|\partial_tY_n\|_{L^{4/3}([0,T];H^{-1})}
  \Big]
  \leq C_T .
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-energy-compactness-bound}
\end{align}
Consequently, after passing to a subsequence, there is a function \(Y\) such
that
\[
\begin{aligned}
  Y_n&\rightharpoonup^\ast Y
    &&\text{weakly-* in }L^\infty([0,T];L^2(\mathbb T^2)),\\
  Y_n&\rightharpoonup Y
    &&\text{weakly in }L^2([0,T];H^1(\mathbb T^2)),\\
  Y_n&\rightharpoonup Y
    &&\text{weakly in }L^4(Q_T),
\end{aligned}
\]
and, after a further subsequence if necessary,
\[
  Y_n(T)\rightharpoonup Y(T)
  \qquad\text{weakly in }L^2(\mathbb T^2),
\]
where \(Y\) is represented by its weakly \(L^2\)-continuous time
representative.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Apply
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-smooth-energy-estimate} on every interval \([0,t]\),
\(0\leq t\leq T\).  Since the right hand side is bounded uniformly by the
definition of \(M_T\), the \(L^\infty_tL^2_x\), \(L^2_tH^1_x\), and
\(L^4_{t,x}\) parts of
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-energy-compactness-bound} follow.

It remains to prove the time-derivative bound.  Let
\(\|v\|_{H^1(\mathbb T^2)}\leq1\).  In two dimensions the Sobolev embedding
\(H^1(\mathbb T^2)\hookrightarrow L^4(\mathbb T^2)\) gives
\[
  \|f\|_{H^{-1}}
  \leq C\|f\|_{L^{4/3}}
  \qquad
  \text{for }f\in L^{4/3}(\mathbb T^2).
\]
Testing the smooth equation against \(v\) therefore gives, in the dual
space \(H^{-1}\),
\begin{equation}
\begin{aligned}
  \|\partial_tY_n\|_{H^{-1}}
  \leq C\Big(
    &\|Y_n\|_{H^1}
     +\|Y_n\|_{L^2}
     +\|Y_n\|_{L^4}^3
     +\|Y_n\|_{L^4}^2\|X_{1,n}\|_{L^4}
\\
    &+\|Y_n\|_{L^4}\|X_{2,n}\|_{L^2}
     +\|X_{3,n}\|_{L^{4/3}}
  \Big).
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-time-derivative-pointwise-bound}
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
Here and below the spatial norms are taken on \(\mathbb T^2\) at the fixed
time under consideration.  Taking the \(L^{4/3}\)-norm in time and using
Hölder's inequality on \(Q_T\) gives
\[
\begin{aligned}
  \|Y_n^3\|_{L^{4/3}(Q_T)}
     &=\|Y_n\|_{L^4(Q_T)}^3,\\
  \|Y_n^2X_{1,n}\|_{L^{4/3}(Q_T)}
     &\leq
       \|Y_n\|_{L^4(Q_T)}^2
       \|X_{1,n}\|_{L^4(Q_T)},\\
  \|Y_nX_{2,n}\|_{L^{4/3}(Q_T)}
     &\leq
       \|Y_n\|_{L^4(Q_T)}
       \|X_{2,n}\|_{L^2(Q_T)} .
\end{aligned}
\]
The already established energy bounds and the definition of \(M_T\) now
prove the \(L^{4/3}_tH^{-1}_x\) estimate for \(\partial_tY_n\).

The compactness assertions are consequences of standard weak compactness,
but we spell out the endpoint identification because it is the point needed
in Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-energy-closed-under-approximation}.
The spaces \(L^2([0,T];H^1)\) and \(L^4(Q_T)\) are reflexive, while
\(L^\infty([0,T];L^2)\) is the dual of \(L^1([0,T];L^2)\) because
\(L^2(\mathbb T^2)\) is separable.  Banach--Alaoglu and a diagonal
subsequence therefore give the displayed weak and weak-* convergences.
The derivative bound passes to the same limit in
\(\mathcal D'((0,T);H^{-1})\), so the limit has an
\(H^{-1}\)-valued absolutely continuous representative.

Let \(D\subset H^1(\mathbb T^2)\) be countable and dense in \(L^2\).  For
each \(v\in D\), the scalar functions
\[
  a_{n,v}(t):=\langle Y_n(t),v\rangle_{L^2}
\]
are bounded in \(W^{1,4/3}([0,T])\): the \(L^\infty_tL^2_x\) bound controls
\(a_{n,v}\), and the \(L^{4/3}_tH^{-1}_x\) bound controls its derivative.
The compact embedding
\(W^{1,4/3}([0,T])\hookrightarrow C([0,T])\), together with the
distributional convergence already obtained, implies
\[
  \langle Y_n(T),v\rangle_{L^2}
  \longrightarrow
  \langle Y(T),v\rangle
  \qquad(v\in D)
\]
after a diagonal extraction.  The sequence \(Y_n(T)\) is bounded in
\(L^2\), so a further subsequence has a weak \(L^2\) limit; the preceding
identity on the dense set \(D\) identifies that limit with \(Y(T)\).  Hence
\(Y_n(T)\rightharpoonup Y(T)\) weakly in \(L^2(\mathbb T^2)\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Fourier Aubin--Lions compactness on the torus]
\label{lem:spde-fourier-aubin-lions}
Let \((u_n)\) be a sequence such that
\[
  \sup_n
  \left[
    \|u_n\|_{L^2([0,T];H^1(\mathbb T^2))}
    +
    \|\partial_tu_n\|_{L^{4/3}([0,T];H^{-1}(\mathbb T^2))}
  \right]
  <\infty .
\]
Then a subsequence converges strongly in
\(L^2([0,T];L^2(\mathbb T^2))\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Let \(P_M\) denote the Fourier projection onto modes \(|k|\leq M\).  The
high-frequency tail is uniformly small:
\[
  \|(1-P_M)u_n\|_{L^2_tL^2_x}
  \leq
  (1+M^2)^{-1/2}
  \|u_n\|_{L^2_tH^1_x}.
\]
It remains to prove compactness of \(P_Mu_n\) for fixed \(M\).  The range
\(E_M=P_ML^2(\mathbb T^2)\) is finite dimensional.  On \(E_M\) all Hilbert
norms are equivalent, and the hypotheses imply
\[
  \sup_n
  \left[
    \|P_Mu_n\|_{L^2([0,T];E_M)}
    +
    \|\partial_tP_Mu_n\|_{L^{4/3}([0,T];E_M)}
  \right]
  <\infty .
\]
Since \([0,T]\) has finite measure, the first term also bounds
\(\|P_Mu_n\|_{L^{4/3}([0,T];E_M)}\).  Thus \(P_Mu_n\) is bounded in
\(W^{1,4/3}([0,T];E_M)\).  For \(s,t\in[0,T]\),
\[
  \|P_Mu_n(t)-P_Mu_n(s)\|_{E_M}
  \leq
  |t-s|^{1/4}\,
  \|\partial_tP_Mu_n\|_{L^{4/3}([0,T];E_M)} ,
\]
so the family is equicontinuous.  The \(L^{4/3}\)-bound also gives a
uniform pointwise bound: for each \(n\), choose \(t_n\) with
\[
  \|P_Mu_n(t_n)\|_{E_M}
  \leq
  T^{-3/4}\|P_Mu_n\|_{L^{4/3}([0,T];E_M)} ,
\]
and use the preceding Hölder estimate to bound \(P_Mu_n(t)\) for every
\(t\).  Arzela--Ascoli in the finite-dimensional space \(E_M\) now gives a
subsequence converging in \(C([0,T];E_M)\), hence strongly in
\(L^2([0,T];E_M)\).  Taking \(M=1,2,\ldots\) and using a diagonal
subsequence, the finite-mode parts converge for every \(M\).  The uniform
tail estimate then shows that the same subsequence is Cauchy in
\(L^2([0,T];L^2(\mathbb T^2))\), proving the lemma.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Identification of the DPD distributional limit]
\label{prop:spde-dpd-distributional-limit}
Let the hypotheses of
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-energy-compactness} hold, and in addition
assume that, for some functions \(X_1,X_2,X_3\),
\[
\begin{aligned}
  X_{1,n}&\to X_1
    &&\text{strongly in }L^4(Q_T),\\
  X_{2,n}&\to X_2
    &&\text{strongly in }L^2(Q_T),\\
  X_{3,n}&\to X_3
    &&\text{strongly in }L^{4/3}(Q_T),
\end{aligned}
\]
and that \(Y_n(0)\to Y_0\) strongly in \(L^2(\mathbb T^2)\).  Let \(Y\) be
the subsequential limit supplied by
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-energy-compactness}.  After passing to a
subsequence, \(Y_n\to Y\) strongly in \(L^r(Q_T)\) for every
\(1\leq r<4\).  Moreover \(Y\) satisfies
\begin{equation}
  \partial_tY
  =
  \Delta Y-m^2Y
  -\lambda\bigl(Y^3+3Y^2X_1+3YX_2+X_3\bigr)
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-distributional-limit-equation}
\end{equation}
as an identity in \(\mathcal D'((0,T)\times\mathbb T^2)\).  Equivalently,
for every \(\varphi\in C^\infty([0,T]\times\mathbb T^2)\),
\begin{align}
  &\langle Y(T),\varphi(T)\rangle
  -\langle Y_0,\varphi(0)\rangle
  -\int_0^T\langle Y(t),\partial_t\varphi(t)\rangle\,\dd t
  \notag\\
  &\qquad =
  \int_0^T
  \langle Y(t),\Delta\varphi(t)-m^2\varphi(t)\rangle\,\dd t
  \notag\\
  &\qquad\quad
  -\lambda\int_{Q_T}
  \bigl(Y^3+3Y^2X_1+3YX_2+X_3\bigr)\varphi\,\dd t\,\dd x .
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-distributional-limit-weak-form}
\end{align}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
The compactness lemma applied to \(u_n=Y_n\) gives, after passing to a
subsequence,
\[
  Y_n\to Y
  \qquad\text{strongly in }L^2(Q_T).
\]
The same sequence is bounded in \(L^4(Q_T)\).  Interpolation between
\(L^2\) convergence and the uniform \(L^4\) bound gives strong convergence
in \(L^r(Q_T)\) for every \(2\leq r<4\), and finite measure gives strong
convergence for \(1\leq r<2\) as well.

We next pass the nonlinearities.  The cubic term satisfies
\[
  \|Y_n^3-Y^3\|_{L^1}
  \leq
  C\|Y_n-Y\|_{L^2}
  \bigl(\|Y_n\|_{L^4}^2+\|Y\|_{L^4}^2\bigr)
  \longrightarrow0 .
\]
For the \(Y^2X_1\) term, choose the strong convergence
\(Y_n\to Y\) in \(L^{8/3}(Q_T)\).  Then
\[
  \|Y_n^2-Y^2\|_{L^{4/3}}
  \leq
  C\|Y_n-Y\|_{L^{8/3}}
  \bigl(\|Y_n\|_{L^{8/3}}+\|Y\|_{L^{8/3}}\bigr)
  \longrightarrow0 .
\]
Hence, using Hölder's inequality and the strong \(L^4\) convergence of
\(X_{1,n}\),
\[
\begin{aligned}
  \|Y_n^2X_{1,n}-Y^2X_1\|_{L^1}
  &\leq
  \|Y_n^2-Y^2\|_{L^{4/3}}\|X_{1,n}\|_{L^4}
\\
  &\quad
  +\|Y^2\|_{L^{4/3}}\|X_{1,n}-X_1\|_{L^4}
  \longrightarrow0 .
\end{aligned}
\]
The mixed term with \(X_2\) is simpler:
\[
  \|Y_nX_{2,n}-YX_2\|_{L^1}
  \leq
  \|Y_n-Y\|_{L^2}\|X_{2,n}\|_{L^2}
  +
  \|Y\|_{L^2}\|X_{2,n}-X_2\|_{L^2}
  \longrightarrow0 .
\]
Finally \(X_{3,n}\to X_3\) in \(L^{4/3}(Q_T)\), hence in \(L^1(Q_T)\)
because \(Q_T\) has finite measure.

For every smooth test function \(\varphi\), the smooth equation for \(Y_n\)
gives
\[
\begin{aligned}
  &\langle Y_n(T),\varphi(T)\rangle
  -\langle Y_n(0),\varphi(0)\rangle
  -\int_0^T\langle Y_n,\partial_t\varphi\rangle\,\dd t
\\
  &\qquad =
  \int_0^T
  \langle Y_n,\Delta\varphi-m^2\varphi\rangle\,\dd t
  -\lambda\int_{Q_T}
  \bigl(Y_n^3+3Y_n^2X_{1,n}+3Y_nX_{2,n}+X_{3,n}\bigr)
  \varphi\,\dd t\,\dd x .
\end{aligned}
\]
The initial term converges by the strong \(L^2\) convergence of \(Y_n(0)\).
The terminal term converges by the weak \(L^2\) convergence
\(Y_n(T)\rightharpoonup Y(T)\) from
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-energy-compactness}.  The linear spacetime
terms converge by the strong \(L^2(Q_T)\) convergence of \(Y_n\), and the
nonlinear terms converge by the \(L^1\) convergence just proved.  Passing to
the limit gives
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-distributional-limit-weak-form}, which is precisely
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-distributional-limit-equation} in distributional form.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Compatibility of the Besov local solution with smooth energy approximation]
\label{prop:spde-dpd-besov-energy-compatibility}
Let \(m^2>0\), \(\lambda>0\), and
\[
  0<\kappa<\alpha<2-\kappa .
\]
Let \(T_0>0\), let
\[
  Y_{0,n}\in C^\infty(\mathbb T^2),
  \qquad
  Y_0\in\mathcal C^\alpha(\mathbb T^2),
\]
with \(Y_{0,n}\to Y_0\) in \(\mathcal C^\alpha\), and let
\[
  \mathbb X_n=(X_{1,n},X_{2,n},X_{3,n})
  \in C^\infty([0,T_0]\times\mathbb T^2)^3
\]
converge to
\(\mathbb X=(X_1,X_2,X_3)\) in
\[
  C([0,T_0];\mathcal C^{-\kappa}(\mathbb T^2))^3 .
\]
Let \(Y_n\) be the smooth solution of
\[
  \partial_tY_n
  =
  (\Delta-m^2)Y_n
  -\lambda\bigl(
    Y_n^3+3Y_n^2X_{1,n}+3Y_nX_{2,n}+X_{3,n}
  \bigr),
  \qquad
  Y_n(0)=Y_{0,n}.
\]
Let \(Y\) be the Besov--Holder mild solution obtained from
Theorem~\ref{thm:spde-dpd-besov-local-fixed-point} for the data
\((Y_0,\mathbb X)\).  Then there is a time \(T\in(0,T_0]\), depending only
on a bounded neighborhood of the convergent data, such that
\begin{equation}
  Y_n\longrightarrow Y
  \qquad\text{in }C([0,T];\mathcal C^\alpha).
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-besov-energy-compatibility-local-convergence}
\end{equation}

Assume in addition that, on \(Q_T=[0,T]\times\mathbb T^2\),
\[
\begin{aligned}
  X_{1,n}&\to X_1
    &&\text{in }L^4(Q_T),\\
  X_{2,n}&\to X_2
    &&\text{in }L^2(Q_T),\\
  X_{3,n}&\to X_3
    &&\text{in }L^{4/3}(Q_T),
\end{aligned}
\]
and that
\[
  \sup_n
  \left[
    \|Y_{0,n}\|_{L^2}
    +\|X_{1,n}\|_{L^4(Q_T)}
    +\|X_{2,n}\|_{L^2(Q_T)}
    +\|X_{3,n}\|_{L^{4/3}(Q_T)}
  \right]
  <\infty .
\]
Then \(Y\) is the energy-compactness limit of the same smooth
approximations.  More precisely, \(Y\in L^2([0,T];H^1)\cap L^4(Q_T)\) and
for every \(0\leq\tau\leq T\),
\begin{align}
  \frac12\|Y(\tau)\|_{L^2}^2
  &+
  \int_0^\tau
  \left[
    \|\nabla Y(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +m^2\|Y(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +\frac{\lambda}{4}\|Y(t)\|_{L^4}^4
  \right]\dd t
  \notag\\
  &\leq
  \frac12\|Y_0\|_{L^2}^2
  +
  C\lambda
  \int_0^\tau
  \left[
    \|X_1(t)\|_{L^4}^4
    +\|X_2(t)\|_{L^2}^2
    +\|X_3(t)\|_{L^{4/3}}^{4/3}
  \right]\dd t .
  \label{eq:spde-dpd-besov-energy-compatibility-energy}
\end{align}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Choose \(R\) so that, for all sufficiently large \(n\),
\[
  \|Y_{0,n}\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}
  +\|Y_0\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}
  +\|\mathbb X_n\|_{C([0,T_0];\mathcal C^{-\kappa})}
  +\|\mathbb X\|_{C([0,T_0];\mathcal C^{-\kappa})}
  \leq R .
\]
The local Lipschitz part of
Theorem~\ref{thm:spde-dpd-besov-local-fixed-point} gives a common time
\(T\in(0,T_0]\) and a Lipschitz constant \(L_R\) on this ball.  The smooth
solution \(Y_n\) satisfies the Duhamel formula with ordinary products.
Since Bony's product agrees with ordinary multiplication on smooth inputs,
this is the same fixed-point equation as
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-mild-equation} for the data
\((Y_{0,n},\mathbb X_n)\).  The uniqueness statement in
Theorem~\ref{thm:spde-dpd-besov-local-fixed-point} therefore identifies
the smooth solution with the Besov fixed point on \([0,T]\).  The Lipschitz
estimate gives
\[
  \|Y_n-Y\|_{C([0,T];\mathcal C^\alpha)}
  \leq
  L_R
  \left(
    \|Y_{0,n}-Y_0\|_{\mathcal C^\alpha}
    +
    \max_i
    \|X_{i,n}-X_i\|_{C([0,T];\mathcal C^{-\kappa})}
  \right),
\]
which proves
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-energy-compatibility-local-convergence}.

For the energy statement, fix \(\tau\in[0,T]\).  The additional hypotheses
on \([0,\tau]\) are exactly the hypotheses of
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-energy-compactness}, so every subsequence of
\((Y_n)\) has a further subsequence converging weakly in
\[
  L^2([0,\tau];H^1),\qquad L^4([0,\tau]\times\mathbb T^2),
  \qquad\text{and at time }\tau\text{ in }L^2.
\]
But the already proved convergence in \(C([0,T];\mathcal C^\alpha)\)
implies convergence in \(C([0,T];L^2)\), because
\(\mathbb T^2\) has finite volume and
\(\mathcal C^\alpha\hookrightarrow L^\infty\hookrightarrow L^2\).
Thus every such weak limit is the same function \(Y\).  In particular
\[
  Y\in L^2([0,\tau];H^1)\cap L^4([0,\tau]\times\mathbb T^2).
\]
Applying
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-energy-closed-under-approximation} on
\([0,\tau]\) gives
\eqref{eq:spde-dpd-besov-energy-compatibility-energy}.  Since \(\tau\) was
arbitrary, the inequality holds for every \(\tau\leq T\).
\end{proof}

The logical role of this proposition is to identify the two approximation
mechanisms on their common domain of validity.  The Besov topology defines
the canonical rough products and gives a unique local mild solution; the
Lebesgue hypotheses are exactly those needed by the elementary energy
inequality.  For the Wick-enhanced stochastic convolution in two dimensions,
the corresponding global theorem requires a rough energy-continuity estimate
in which the Lebesgue pairings above are replaced by the appropriate
paracontrolled or Besov energy pairings.

The rough Da Prato--Debussche global argument is therefore reduced to two
precise analytic tasks.  First, construct smooth approximations whose
enhanced noises converge in the integrability spaces appearing in
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-energy-closed-under-approximation}, or in
Besov/paracontrolled spaces that imply those bounds after pairing with the
energy solution.  Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-distributional-limit}
then identifies the compactness limit as a distributional solution whenever
the products are represented in these strong \(L^p\) spaces.  In the full
rough stochastic application, where the enhanced noise is controlled in
Besov or paracontrolled coordinates rather than in ordinary Lebesgue spaces,
the same role is played, at the elementary Besov level, by
Proposition~\ref{prop:spde-dpd-besov-product-continuity}.  The quartic
term supplies the coercive bound, compactness extracts a weak energy limit,
product continuity identifies the equation in the topology where the
enhanced noise is built, and the closedness proposition passes the energy
inequality to that rough limit.

\subsection{Global Continuity, Invariant Laws, and Tightness}
\label{subsec:spde-dpd-global-invariant-tightness}

\begin{lemma}[Global compact-continuity criterion for DPD solution maps]
\label{lem:spde-dpd-global-compact-continuity-criterion}
Fix \(T>0\), \(0<\kappa<1\), and an exponent
\(\alpha\in(0,2-\kappa)\) for which the local Besov fixed-point theorem
applies.  Let
\[
  \mathfrak D_T
  :=(\mathcal C^\alpha(\mathbb T^2)\cap L^2(\mathbb T^2))
    \times C([0,T];\mathcal C^{-\kappa}(\mathbb T^2))^3
\]
with its product topology, and write a datum as
\[
  D=(Y_0,\mathbb X_1,\mathbb X_2,\mathbb X_3).
\]
For each \(D\) let \(Y_D\) be the maximal local DPD solution produced by
Theorem~\ref{thm:spde-dpd-besov-local-fixed-point}, and denote its maximal
existence time in \([0,T]\) by \(\tau(D)\).

Let \(K\subset\mathfrak D_T\) be compact.  Assume the following two
statements hold for every \(D\in K\).
\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Blow-up alternative.}  If \(\tau(D)<T\), then
\[
  \limsup_{t\uparrow\tau(D)}
  \|Y_D\|_{C([0,t];\mathcal C^\alpha)}=\infty .
\]
\item \emph{Rough energy-to-Besov bound.}  There is a finite constant
\(B_{K,T}\), depending on \(K\) and \(T\) but not on \(D\), such that
\[
  \sup_{0<s<\tau(D)}
  \|Y_D\|_{C([0,s];\mathcal C^\alpha)}
  \leq B_{K,T}.
\]
\end{enumerate}
Then every \(D\in K\) has a solution on the full interval \([0,T]\), and
the solution map
\[
  \mathcal S_T:K\longrightarrow C([0,T];\mathcal C^\alpha),
  \qquad D\longmapsto Y_D,
\]
is uniformly continuous.  Consequently, if \(D_N\to D\) in
\(\mathfrak D_T\), and if \(\{D\}\cup\{D_N:N\geq N_0\}\) is contained in a
compact set on which the two hypotheses above hold with a common constant,
then
\[
  Y_{D_N}\longrightarrow Y_D
  \quad\hbox{in }C([0,T];\mathcal C^\alpha).
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
The full-time existence statement is the contradiction between the two
assumptions.  If \(\tau(D)<T\), the rough energy-to-Besov estimate gives a
finite bound for
\(\|Y_D\|_{C([0,s];\mathcal C^\alpha)}\) as \(s\uparrow\tau(D)\), whereas
